Classification of Sensible Accidents
The author lists the five types of sensible accidents by sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam philosophers classify sensible accidents according to the five external senses.

Core Idea
The five senses define the types of sensible accidents.

This passage begins the classification of accidents. The first division splits into accidents perceived by the senses and the akwan. Here we examine the five categories of sensible accidents, each tied to a specific sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of sensible accidents
Read the Arabic passage listing the five categories. Notice that each category corresponds to a sense: sight, hearing, taste, smell, and touch.
The author uses the phrase 'al-mudrakah bi al-quwwah al-baṣirah' etc.
2
Identify each sense and its objects
List each sense and the specific accidents it perceives. For example, sight perceives colors and lights; hearing perceives letters and sounds; taste perceives nine tastes; smell perceives odors; touch perceives heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and their combinations.
The nine tastes are a standard list in kalam.
3
Ask: Why is this classification important?
This classification helps in understanding how accidents are known and how they are used in arguments about the physical world. It also sets the stage for discussing non-sensible accidents (akwan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسم الأول : فنوعان : أحدهما : الأعراض المحسوسة بإحدى الحواس الخمس إحساساً أولياً والثاني : الأكوان .. أما القسم الأول : فعلى خمسة أقسام : فالأول : المدركة بالقوة الباصرة ، وهي الألوان والأضواء . والثاني : المدركة بالقوة السامعة ، وهي الحروف والأصوات . والثالث : المدركة بالقوة الذائقة ، وهي الطعوم التسعة . والرابع : المدركة بالقوة الشامة وهي الروائح . والخامس : المدركة بالقوة اللامسة ، وهي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ، وما يتولد من إجتماعهما . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو الأكوان : فالكون عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في الحيز . ثم إن الكون جنس تحته أربعة أنواع : فالأول : الحركة . وهي الحصول الأول في الحيز . والثاني : السكون : وهو عبارة عن الحصول الثاني في الحيز الأول . والثالث : الإجتماع وهو عبارة عن حصول متحيزين في حيزين بحيث لا يتخللهما فضاء وخلاء . والرابع : الإفتراق . وهو عبارة عن حصول متحيزين في حيزين بحيث يتخللهما فضاء وخلاء .
As for the first division, it is two types: one of them: accidents perceived by one of the five senses with primary sensation, and the second: the akwan. As for the first type (sensible accidents), it is of five divisions: the first: what is perceived by the power of sight, namely colors and lights. The second: what is perceived by the power of hearing, namely letters and sounds. The third: what is perceived by the power of taste, namely the nine tastes. The fourth: what is perceived by the power of smell, namely odors. And the fifth: what is perceived by the power of touch, namely heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and what is generated from their combination. As for the second type, namely the akwan: al-kawn (state) is the occurrence of a substance (jawhar) in a location (hayyiz). Then al-kawn is a genus under which are four species: the first: motion (harakah), which is the first occurrence in a location. The second: rest (sukūn), which is the second occurrence in the first location. The third: conjunction (ijtimā‘), which is the occurrence of two located things in two locations such that no space or void intervenes between them. And the fourth: separation (iftirāq), which is the occurrence of two located things in two locations such that space or void intervenes between them.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage classifies the first division of accidents (a‘rāḍ) into two types: sensible accidents and akwan. Sensible accidents are further divided into five categories corresponding to the five external senses: sight (colors and lights), hearing (letters and sounds), taste (nine tastes), smell (odors), and touch (heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and their combinations). The second type, akwan, are defined as the state of a substance (jawhar) in a location (ḥayyiz). Al-kawn is a genus with four species: motion (first occurrence in a location), rest (second occurrence in the same location), conjunction (two located things in two locations with no intervening space), and separation (two located things in two locations with intervening space). This classification is fundamental in kalām physics.

القوة السامعة
القوة الذائقة
القوة الشامة
القوة اللامسة

Key Takeaway
Sensible accidents are classified into five categories corresponding to the five external senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that your senses are limited to five types of accidents. Which of these five senses do you take most for granted, and how would your awareness of Allah's creation change if you lost that sense today? Make a specific plan to use that sense to see a sign of Allah before the day ends.
?
The author lists 'letters and sounds' as objects of hearing. When you hear the Qur'an recited, do you perceive it merely as sounds, or as a divine message? What is one practical change you can make to listen more attentively as an act of worship?
?
Touch perceives heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and combinations. Ponder on the fact that your every physical comfort or discomfort is a created accident. How does that realization humble you before the Creator of these accidents, and what is one way you can express gratitude for the sense of touch today?

The Akwan: Motion, Rest, Conjunction, and Separation
The author defines al-kawn and enumerates its four species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-kawn and its four species in kalam physics.

Core Idea
Akwan describe the states of a substance in space: motion, rest, conjunction, separation.

After sensible accidents, the author turns to the second type: akwan. These are not directly sensed but are inferred states of substances in locations. The passage defines al-kawn and breaks it into four species.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-kawn
Read the Arabic: 'al-kawn ʿibāratun ʿan ḥuṣūli al-jawhari fī al-ḥayyiz'. Note that al-kawn is defined as the occurrence of a substance in a location.
Jawhar (substance) and hayyiz (location) are key terms.
2
Understand the four species
The four species under al-kawn are: motion (first occurrence in a location), rest (second occurrence in the first location), conjunction (two substances with no space between), and separation (two substances with space between).
Motion and rest are temporal states; conjunction and separation are spatial relations.
3
Relate to physical reality
Think of a substance moving from one place to another: the first moment it arrives is motion; if it stays, that is rest. Two substances can be together (conjunction) or apart (separation). This classification is foundational for kalam cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القسم الأول : فنوعان : أحدهما : الأعراض المحسوسة بإحدى الحواس الخمس إحساساً أولياً والثاني : الأكوان .. أما القسم الأول : فعلى خمسة أقسام : فالأول : المدركة بالقوة الباصرة ، وهي الألوان والأضواء . والثاني : المدركة بالقوة السامعة ، وهي الحروف والأصوات . والثالث : المدركة بالقوة الذائقة ، وهي الطعوم التسعة . والرابع : المدركة بالقوة الشامة وهي الروائح . والخامس : المدركة بالقوة اللامسة ، وهي الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ، وما يتولد من إجتماعهما . وأما القسم الثاني : وهو الأكوان : فالكون عبارة عن حصول الجوهر في الحيز . ثم إن الكون جنس تحته أربعة أنواع : فالأول : الحركة . وهي الحصول الأول في الحيز . والثاني : السكون : وهو عبارة عن الحصول الثاني في الحيز الأول . والثالث : الإجتماع وهو عبارة عن حصول متحيزين في حيزين بحيث لا يتخللهما فضاء وخلاء . والرابع : الإفتراق . وهو عبارة عن حصول متحيزين في حيزين بحيث يتخللهما فضاء وخلاء .
As for the first division, it is two types: one of them: accidents perceived by one of the five senses with primary sensation, and the second: the akwan. As for the first type (sensible accidents), it is of five divisions: the first: what is perceived by the power of sight, namely colors and lights. The second: what is perceived by the power of hearing, namely letters and sounds. The third: what is perceived by the power of taste, namely the nine tastes. The fourth: what is perceived by the power of smell, namely odors. And the fifth: what is perceived by the power of touch, namely heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and what is generated from their combination. As for the second type, namely the akwan: al-kawn (state) is the occurrence of a substance (jawhar) in a location (hayyiz). Then al-kawn is a genus under which are four species: the first: motion (harakah), which is the first occurrence in a location. The second: rest (sukūn), which is the second occurrence in the first location. The third: conjunction (ijtimā‘), which is the occurrence of two located things in two locations such that no space or void intervenes between them. And the fourth: separation (iftirāq), which is the occurrence of two located things in two locations such that space or void intervenes between them.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage classifies the first division of accidents (a‘rāḍ) into two types: sensible accidents and akwan. Sensible accidents are further divided into five categories corresponding to the five external senses: sight (colors and lights), hearing (letters and sounds), taste (nine tastes), smell (odors), and touch (heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and their combinations). The second type, akwan, are defined as the state of a substance (jawhar) in a location (ḥayyiz). Al-kawn is a genus with four species: motion (first occurrence in a location), rest (second occurrence in the same location), conjunction (two located things in two locations with no intervening space), and separation (two located things in two locations with intervening space). This classification is fundamental in kalām physics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own spiritual 'motion' and 'rest'—your coming to faith and your staying in that first location—are you truly in a state of 'rest' as defined here: a second occurrence in the same location? Or have you moved away? What does it mean to be connected without void to your Creator?
?
Reflect on your relationships. Is there any relationship where there is a void (space) between you and another? What steps can you take toward conjunction—closing that void—as defined by this lesson?

Accidents Requiring Life and the Taxonomy of Beliefs
Sanusi classifies accidents requiring life and the hierarchy of beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of accidents that require a living being, and the detailed hierarchy of beliefs within Islamic epistemology, including concepts, assents, certainty, conformity, and epistemic sources.

Core Idea
Beliefs are classified by certainty, conformity, and source of knowledge, forming a map of human perception.

This passage by Sanusi lays out the foundational classification of attributes that can only exist in living beings, focusing on the realm of perception. Within perception, he distinguishes between beliefs (i'tiqādāt) and sensory perceptions, and then provides a detailed taxonomy of beliefs based on their certainty, conformity to reality, and epistemic source. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for navigating later discussions in kalam about knowledge, doubt, and faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main divisions
Read the first part of the passage that distinguishes the second kind of accident as requiring life, and note its subdivisions: life itself and attributes conditional upon life.
2
Explore the attributes of perception
Understand that perception attributes are of two types: beliefs and sensory perceptions. Focus on the belief branch, which is further divided into concepts and assents.
Beliefs are either concepts (tasawwur) or assents (tasdiq).
3
Classify assents by certainty
Assents are either certain (jāzim) or non-certain. Certain assents are either conforming or non-conforming to reality.
Certainty here means firm conviction.
4
Distinguish sources of conforming assents
Conforming assents come from necessity (sensory or primary), theoretical reasoning, or tradition. Understand each source.
Primary necessary assents are self-evident.
5
Map non-certain and non-conforming beliefs
Non-conforming certain belief is compound ignorance. Non-certain beliefs include doubt, opinion, and illusion. Note the balances.
Opinion (zann) and illusion (wahm) are based on preponderance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني من قسمي العرض ، فهو العرض الذي يمتنع قيامه إلا بالحي . وهو أقسام : فأحدهما : الحياة ، وثانيهما: الصفات التي تكون مشروطة بالحياة وهي قسمان: أحدهما: الصفات المعتبرة في الإدراك . والثاني الصفات المعتبرة في الفعل . أما الصفات التي تفيد الإدراك فقسمان : الأول : الإعتقادات وهي إما تصورات أو تصديقات . والتصديقات : إما أن تكون جازمة أو غير جازمة . أما الجازمة فإما مطابقة أو غير مطابقة ، والمطابقة إما أن تكون علوماً ضرورية أو نظرية أو تقليدية . أما الضروريات . فإما أن تكون مستفادة من إحدى الحواس الخمس ، وإما أن تكون أولية بمعنى أن مجرد تصور موضوعها ومحمولها ، يوجب التصديق بإسناد محمولها إلى موضوعها ، أو بسلبه عنه . وأما النظريات فمعلومة ، وأما التقليديات فهو أن يعتقد الإنسان إعتقاداً جازماً في الشيء من غير دليل ولا شبهة . وأما الإعتقاد الجازم الذي لا يكون مطابقاً ، فهو الجهل المركب ، وأما الاعتقاد الذي لا يكون جازماً فإن كان التردد في الطرفين على السوية ، فهو الشك ، وإن كان أحدهما أقوى من الآخر ، فالراجح هو الظن ، والمرجوح هو الوهم . والقسم الثاني من هذا الباب : الإدراكات الحاصلة بالحواس الخمسة ، وهي معلومة .
As for the second division of the two divisions of accident, it is the accident that cannot subsist except in a living being. And it has divisions: one of them is life (ḥayāh), and the second are the attributes that are conditional upon life, and they are two kinds: one of them: attributes considered in perception (idrāk), and the second: attributes considered in action (fi‘l). As for the attributes that provide perception, they are two kinds: the first: beliefs (i‘tiqādāt), and they are either concepts (taṣawwurāt) or assents (taṣdīqāt). And the assents are either certain (jāzim) or non-certain. As for the certain, they are either conforming (muṭābiq) or non-conforming. And the conforming are either necessary (ḍarūrī) sciences, or theoretical (naẓarī), or traditional (taqlīdī). As for the necessary sciences, they are either derived from one of the five senses, or they are primary (awwalī) in the sense that mere conception of their subject and predicate necessitates assent to the attribution of the predicate to the subject, or its negation from it. As for the theoretical sciences, they are known. As for the traditional sciences, it is that a person holds a certain belief about something without proof or semblance of proof. As for the certain belief that is not conforming, it is compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). As for the belief that is not certain, if the hesitation between the two sides is equal, it is doubt (shakk). If one of them is stronger than the other, the preponderant is opinion (ẓann) and the preponderated is illusion (wahm). And the second kind of this section are the perceptions (idrākāt) obtained through the five senses, and they are known.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage presents the second division of accidents: those that cannot subsist except in a living being. This division includes life (ḥayāh) and qualities conditional upon life, which are of two types: those considered in perception (idrāk) and those considered in action (fi‘l). Here the focus is on perception, which is divided into beliefs (i‘tiqādāt) and sensory perceptions. Beliefs are either concepts (taṣawwurāt) or assents (taṣdīqāt). Assents are further classified by certainty: certain (jāzim) or non-certain. Certain assents are either conforming (muṭābiq) to reality or non-conforming. Conforming assents are either necessary (ḍarūrī), theoretical (naẓarī), or traditional (taqlīdī). Necessary sciences are either derived from the senses or are primary (awwalī) where mere conception yields assent. Theoretical sciences require reasoning, and traditional sciences are accepted without proof. Non-conforming certain belief is compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). Non-certain belief, if equally balanced, is doubt (shakk); if one side preponderates, it is opinion (ẓann) or illusion (wahm). Finally, sensory perceptions are mentioned briefly as the second kind of perception.

Key Terms

إدراك
اعتقاد
تصور
تصديق
جازم
مطابق
ضروري
نظري
تقليدي
جهل مركب

وهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Traditional (taqlīdī) beliefs are necessarily invalid or blameworthy.
Correction: The passage defines traditional sciences as certain beliefs held without proof or semblance of proof. They may be valid if the source of authority is reliable, though they are not arrived at through personal reasoning.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'compound ignorance' (jahl murakkab) according to this passage?
A) Belief that is not certain and not conforming
B) Certain belief that is not conforming to reality
C) Hesitating between two equal sides
D) Accepting a belief from tradition without proof

Key Takeaway
This passage provides a comprehensive map of human beliefs, distinguishing them by certainty, conformity, and epistemic source, which is foundational for kalam epistemology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which category of belief do you most often operate from—necessary, theoretical, or traditional? How does that shape the strength of your faith when you face intellectual challenges?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain about a belief, only to discover later that it did not conform to reality? How did you confront that compound ignorance, and what did it teach you about your own epistemic humility?
?
When you are in a state of doubt (shakk) about a matter of creed, do you actively seek to strengthen the preponderant side (ẓann) or do you remain passive? What concrete step can you take today to move from doubt toward greater certainty in your faith?

Defining Iftiraq: Separation by Space
Sanusi defines the fourth kind of separation (iftiraq).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam definition of iftiraq (separation) as the occurrence of two bodies in two distinct locations with an intervening space or void.

Core Idea
Iftiraq is not just difference but spatial separation with a gap.

In kalam, bodies are not only characterized by their own attributes but also by their relationships to one another. One such relationship is separation (iftiraq). This lesson presents the precise definition given by the author, distinguishing it from mere conceptual difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the key terms: الإفتراق, متحيزين, حيزين, فضاء, خلاء.
The definition is concise; each word carries technical weight.
2
Understand the components
The definition has three parts: (1) two bodies (متحيزين), (2) two locations (حيزين), and (3) the condition that space/void intervenes between them.
The term متحيز refers to a body occupying space.
3
Contrast with other separations
Consider how iftiraq differs from other kinds of separation (e.g., conceptual separation). This definition emphasizes a physical gap.
The misconception is that separation can occur without a spatial gap; this definition refutes that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والرابع : الإفتراق . وهو عبارة عن حصول متحيزين في حيزين بحيث يتخللهما فضاء وخلاء .
And the fourth: al-iftiraq. It is the occurrence of two bodies (mutahayyizayn) in two locations such that space and void intervene between them.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth kind of separation (iftiraq) among bodies. The author states that iftiraq is when two bodies are in two separate locations with space and void in between. This is one of the divisions of bodies in kalam.

Key Terms
إفتراق
متحيز
حيز
فضاء
خلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iftiraq refers to separation without a spatial gap, e.g., two bodies being separate in concept but not necessarily in location.
Correction: The passage explicitly requires that space or void intervenes between the two bodies, meaning they must occupy distinct locations with a physical gap.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of iftiraq according to the passage?
A) The merging of two bodies
B) Two bodies in one location
C) Two bodies in two locations with space between
D) The non-existence of bodies

Key Takeaway
Iftiraq is defined as two bodies in two separate locations with space or void between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there a 'void' (khalā') between your daily actions and the state of your heart—something you know should be connected but isn't? Identify one such gap and name one concrete step to close it today.
?
Just as iftiraq requires two bodies in separate places, have you separated your knowledge of this true definition from your actual practice? Where in your life today will you intentionally occupy the same physical space as obedience, rather than letting distance creep in?

The Third Division: Reflection and Contemplation
Introduces the third division of accidents and its disputed classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute about whether reflection and contemplation are a type of belief or a distinct category.

Core Idea
Reflection may be a distinct accident, not merely a belief.

After discussing earlier divisions of accidents, the author turns to the third: reflection and contemplation. These are cognitive acts, but their classification is debated. This passage introduces that dispute, setting the stage for a deeper inquiry into the nature of rational thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third division. Notice the precise wording of the dispute.
2
Identify the two positions
The dispute is whether reflection is of the same genus as beliefs or a separate genus. Identify what is at stake in this classification.
This affects how we understand the relationship between thought and belief.
3
Consider the implications
Ask yourself: if reflection is a type of belief, then all rational thought is belief-based. If distinct, then reflection has its own essence. How might this impact theology?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثالث من هذا الباب : النظر والتفكر . وفيه خلاف في أنه هل هو من جنس الإعتقادات ، أو هو قسم مغاير لها ؟ والقسم الرابع : الألم واللذة . وفيه خلاف . وهو أن الألم هل هو نفس إدراك المنافي ، أو حالة زائدة ؟ وأما الصفات التي تفيد المكنة من الفعل، فهي أنواع: الأول : القدرة ، والثاني : الإرادة والكراهية : والثالث : الشهوة والنفرة . وفيه خلاف في أن الشهوة والنفرة هل هما نفس الإرادة والكراهة ، أو جنسان مغايران للإرادة والكراهة ؟ فهذا هو الإشارة المختصرة إلى نوع الأعراض
The third section of this chapter: reflection and contemplation. And there is disagreement about whether it is of the same genus as beliefs, or a distinct section. The fourth section: pain and pleasure. And there is disagreement about it: whether pain is the perception of the contrary itself, or an additional state. As for the attributes that enable action, they are of types: first: power; second: will and aversion; third: appetite and repulsion. And there is disagreement about appetite and repulsion: whether they are the same as will and aversion, or two genera distinct from will and aversion. So this is the brief indication of the type of accidents.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a classification of accidents (a'rāḍ) and active powers. It begins by listing the third and fourth sections of the chapter under discussion: reflection/contemplation (al-nazar wa al-tafakkur) and pain/pleasure (al-alam wa al-ladhdha). For each, it notes a dispute: whether reflection is a type of belief or distinct, and whether pain is identical to the perception of a harmful thing or an additional state. Then it discusses attributes that enable action: power (qudra), will/aversion (irāda/karāha), and appetite/repulsion (shahwa/nafra), again noting the dispute about whether appetite and repulsion are identical to will and aversion or different genera. The passage concludes that this is a brief indication of the types of accidents.

Key Terms
نظر
تفكر
اعتقادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think reflection is obviously a belief, so the dispute is trivial.
Correction: The passage shows that this is a genuine dispute among scholars, implying that reflection may have a different essential nature than belief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the dispute regarding reflection and contemplation?
A) Whether they are created or uncreated
B) Whether they are of the same genus as beliefs or a distinct section
C) Whether they are necessary or contingent
D) Whether they are physical or spiritual

Key Takeaway
Reflection and contemplation may be a distinct class of accident, not reducible to belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Does your personal experience align with reflection being a kind of belief, or a separate process? And what might be at stake if you get it wrong—in your search for truth or in your relationship with Allah?

The Fourth Division: Pain and Pleasure
Presents the fourth division of accidents and the dispute about the nature of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute about whether pain is identical to the perception of a harmful thing or an additional state.

Core Idea
Pain may be more than just perceiving harm; it could be an extra quality.

The author now turns to the fourth division of accidents: pain and pleasure. These are crucial to understanding human experience. The dispute here is whether pain is simply the perception of something harmful, or an additional state that arises from that perception. This lesson unpacks that philosophical question.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the fourth division
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two possible views on pain.
2
Compare the two views
One view: pain is identical to the perception of a contrary (harmful) thing. Another view: pain is an additional state (hāla zā'ida) beyond that perception.
This is similar to the debate about whether physical pain is just a sensation or a judgment.
3
Think about pleasure
The passage mentions pleasure alongside pain, but the dispute is specifically about pain. Consider whether the same analysis could apply to pleasure.

The third section of this chapter: reflection and contemplation. And there is disagreement about whether it is of the same genus as beliefs, or a distinct section. Defining Huduth: Preceded by Non-Existence
Defines huduth and argues for superiority of non-existence interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the most accurate definition of 'originated' (al-muḥdath) is that its existence is preceded by non-existence, and why this definition is more fundamental than being preceded by another existent.

Core Idea
Origination fundamentally requires prior non-existence, not necessarily a prior agent.

Before we can discuss whether the universe is originated, we need a precise definition of what 'originated' means. In this passage, the author argues that the essential meaning of an originated thing is that it is preceded by non-existence, not that it is caused by something else. This definition holds regardless of one's view on causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation to grasp the overall argument before diving into analysis.
Pay attention to the two possible interpretations the author presents.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The author says the originated must be preceded by something: either non-existence or another existent. Find these two options in the text.
3
Understand why non-existence is preferred
Follow the author's reasoning: the core concept of being originated does not require a prior cause, but it does require prior non-existence. This is true even if one denies a cause.
Consider the phrase 'because the originated insofar as it is originated...'
4
Reflect on the implication
Realize that this definition is broad enough to encompass different views on causation, making it a solid foundation for kalam discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية في تفسير الحدوث. نقول : لا شك أنه لا يمكن تفسير المحدث، إلا بأنه الذي سبقه غيره ثم إما أن يكون ذلك السابق عدماً أو وجوداً، فإن كان عدماً فحينئذ يقال : إن المحدث هو الذي يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بالعدم، وإن كان السابق وجوداً فحينئذ يقال : إن المحدث هو الذي سبقه وجود شيء آخر. والتفسير الأول أولى، لأن المحدث من حيث إنه محدث يجب أن يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بعدمه، ولا يجب من حيث هو محدث أن يكون وجوده مسبوقاً بوجود غيره، لأنه مالم يثبت بالدليل أن المحدث لا يحدث إلا لمحدث موجود، فإنه لا يثبت أن المحدث لا بد وأن يكون مسبوقاً بوجود غيره. أما سواء قلنا : إن المحدث يحدث لنفسه أو لغيره، أو لا لنفسه ولا لغيره، فإنه يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم. فيثبت : أن التفسير المحدث بأنه الذي يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم أولى من تفسيره بأنه الذي يجب أن يكون مسبوقاً بغيره. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : قد عرفت في كتاب : أحكام الموجودات : أن السبق والتقدم على أقسام خمسة : أحدها : التقدم بالعلية. وثانيها : بالذات. وثالثها : بالشرف. ورابعها : بالمكان. وخامسها : بالزمان
The Second Introduction: On the interpretation of origination (al-ḥudūth). We say: There is no doubt that 'originated' (al-muḥdath) can only be interpreted as that which is preceded by something else. Then either that predecessor is non-existence or existence. If it is non-existence, then it is said: The originated is that whose existence is preceded by non-existence. And if the predecessor is existence, then it is said: The originated is that which is preceded by the existence of something else. And the first interpretation is more appropriate, because the originated insofar as it is originated must have its existence preceded by its non-existence, and it is not necessary insofar as it is originated that its existence be preceded by the existence of another, because as long as it has not been established by proof that the originated only occurs from a prior existing agent, it is not established that the originated must be preceded by the existence of another. But whether we say that the originated occurs by itself or by another, or not by itself nor by another, it must be preceded by non-existence. Thus it is established that the interpretation of 'originated' as that which must be preceded by non-existence is more appropriate than its interpretation as that which must be preceded by another. When you know this, we say: You have already learned in the book 'Aḥkām al-Mawjūdāt' that priority and precedence are of five divisions: first: priority by causality; second: priority by essence; third: priority by honor; fourth: priority by place; fifth: priority by time.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'originated' (muḥdath) and argues for its correct interpretation. The author begins by stating that 'originated' can only be defined as that which is preceded by something else. That predecessor can be non-existence (ʿadam) or existence (wujūd). If it is preceded by non-existence, it is 'originated'; if preceded by another existent, it is also called 'originated' but the first definition is more appropriate. The reason is that the core concept of being originated requires prior non-existence, regardless of whether it has a prior cause. Even if one denies any external cause, the originated still must be preceded by its own non-existence. The author then references his work 'Aḥkām al-Mawjūdāt' which lists five kinds of priority: causal, essential, honorific, spatial, and temporal. This helps clarify that the priority relevant to ḥudūth is temporal priority over non-existence, not any of the other kinds.

Key Terms
حدوث
محدث
عدم
وجود
سبق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'originated' necessarily implies creation by a prior agent.
Correction: The author emphasizes that origination only requires priority of non-existence, not a prior cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is defining 'originated' as preceded by non-existence more appropriate than as preceded by another existent?
A) Because all originated things are created by God
B) Because the core concept of being originated does not require a prior agent, only prior non-existence
C) Because the philosophers proved it
D) Because it avoids the problem of infinite regress

Key Takeaway
The essential meaning of origination is being preceded by non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist now, but before you were conceived, you were nothing. How does the reality that your entire existence is preceded by non-existence affect your sense of self-importance? Write one specific way you can remind yourself daily that you are not self-made.
?
The passage argues that being preceded by non-existence is the core of origination, not needing a cause. Yet you know from revelation that you are created by God. If someone asked you why you believe you were created, would your answer rely on the fact that you began to exist, or on something deeper? Ponder the difference.
?
If all originated things are preceded by non-existence, then everything you rely on—your health, wealth, relationships—shares that fragility. How does this truth challenge your feeling of security? What is one practical step you can take today to place your ultimate trust in the Eternal rather than the originated?

The Five Kinds of Priority
Lists the five kinds of priority and precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and distinguish the five kinds of priority or precedence as classified in Islamic thought.

Core Idea
Temporal priority is just one of five ways one thing can be prior to another.

After establishing that origination involves priority of non-existence, the author refers to his earlier work which categorizes priority into five types. This classification helps clarify that the kind of priority relevant to origination is temporal priority, not causal, essential, honorific, or spatial priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of five kinds
Read the Arabic and translation for the list. Note that it is a simple enumeration.
The author references his earlier book 'Aḥkām al-Mawjūdāt'.
2
Understand each type
For each of the five, think about what it means for something to be prior in that sense. For example, cause is prior to effect.
The commentary provides brief explanations.
3
Identify which applies to huduth
Reflect on which type of priority is meant when we say the originated is preceded by non-existence. It is temporal priority: the non-existence is prior in time.
4
Memorize the classification
These five types are a useful tool in kalam and philosophy. Try to remember them: causal, essential, honorific, spatial, temporal.

The Five Types of Precedence
Enumeration of five types of precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five distinct types of precedence (sabq/taqaddum) used in Islamic theology and logic.

Core Idea
Precedence has five meanings; confusing them leads to theological errors.

In Islamic thought, the concept of 'precedence' is not one-dimensional. Before delving into definitions of 'hadith', the author recalls a key classification from his earlier work: five types of precedence. This passage lays out that classification clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the enumeration.
2
Identify each type
Notice the author lists five: causal, essential, honorary, spatial, and temporal. Write them down.
3
Think of an example for each
For each type, try to come up with a concrete example from your own understanding.
Causal: fire precedes smoke; Essential: God precedes creation; Honorary: the Prophet precedes common believers; Spatial: the sky precedes the earth; Temporal: yesterday precedes today.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask: Why would a theologian need to distinguish these? Which one is most relevant to defining 'originated'?
The commentary will help you see how this classification is used later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : قد عرفت في كتاب : أحكام الموجودات : أن السبق والتقدم على أقسام خمسة : أحدها : التقدم بالعلية . وثانيها : بالذات . وثالثها : بالشرف . ورابعها : بالمكان. وخامسها : بالزمان.
And if you have understood this, we say: You have already known in the book 'Ahkam al-Mawjudat' (Rulings of Existents) that precedence and priority are of five divisions: First: causal precedence (al-taqaddum bil-ʿilliyyah). Second: essential precedence (bil-dhāt). Third: honorary precedence (bil-sharaf). Fourth: spatial precedence (bil-makān). Fifth: temporal precedence (bil-zamān).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a brief enumeration. The author recalls a classification from his earlier work 'Ahkam al-Mawjudat' identifying five types of precedence (sabq/taqaddum). These are: 1) causal precedence (e.g., the cause precedes the effect), 2) essential precedence (e.g., the one that does not depend on another, like the Necessary Existent precedes contingent in essence), 3) honorary precedence (e.g., the nobler precedes the less noble), 4) spatial precedence (e.g., one object is located above another), and 5) temporal precedence (e.g., one event occurs before another in time). This classification will be used to analyze the definitions of 'al-hadith'.

Key Terms
السبق
التقدم
العلية
الذات
الشرف
المكان
الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think all precedence is temporal or that the types are not distinct.
Correction: These five types are distinct and used in different contexts; temporal precedence is only one of them.

Checkpoint
How many types of precedence does the author list, and what is the fifth type?
A) Four; the fourth is temporal.
B) Five; the fifth is temporal.
C) Five; the fifth is causal.
D) Three; the third is essential.

Key Takeaway
The five types of precedence—causal, essential, honorary, spatial, and temporal—are foundational for later theological discussions on the nature of originated things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that Allah has essential precedence over all creation. Does your daily life reflect that priority, or do you often act as if temporal or honorary precedence matters more? What is one concrete change you can make today to align your priorities with this truth?
?
The author warns that confusing types of precedence leads to theological errors. Have you ever mistakenly applied a temporal or causal understanding to Allah's attributes? How can this classification help you correct your thinking and deepen your awe of Him?
?
Reflect on your own quest for honor: Do you seek precedence in honor from people, or do you seek the essential honor that comes from closeness to the One who has essential precedence over all? What would it take for your heart to truly desire that?

First Saying: Eternal Bodies Devoid of Attributes
Presents the first saying of those who say matter is body, with two possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first saying of those who consider prime matter to be body, including its two main possibilities and the arguments from revelation they use.

Core Idea
The first saying claims bodies were eternal and bare, then attributes arose through division or motion.

This lesson dives into the first of two sayings held by those who identify prime matter with body. It lays out two distinct possibilities for how attributes emerged from a primordial state of bodies without attributes, each supported by scriptural allusions from the Quran and Torah. Understanding these views is essential for grasping the broader debate on the nature of matter in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main possibilities
Read the passage to find the two possibilities under the first saying: (1) all was one connected body that later split, (2) parts were always separate.
The second possibility itself has two sub-possibilities, watch for them.
2
Examine the scriptural evidence
Note how the author uses Quran 21:30 for the first possibility and the beginning of the Torah for the second sub-possibility.
The Torah reference is presented as an allusion, not as authoritative scripture.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider that this saying implies bodies existed eternally without attributes, which differs from the Ash'ari view of temporal creation.
This prepares for the second saying which posits eternal motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذه التفاصيل فنقول: الذين قالوا: المادة الأولى هي الجسم، وزعموا: أن الأجسام متماثلة في تمام الماهية. فهؤلاء لهم قولان: الأول: أن الأجسام كانت موجودة في الأزل، وكانت خالية عن جميع الصفات، ثم إنه حدثت الصفات فيها، وعلى هذا التقدير فهنا أيضاً احتمالان: أحدهما: أن يقال: إن الكل كان جسماً واحداً متصلاً، ثم إنه انفصل البعض عن البعض فيما لا يزال، وهذا هو الذي يشعر به قوله تعالى: أو لم ير الذين كفروا: أن السموات والأرض: كانتا رتقا ففتقناهما: وثانيهما: أن يقال: إن تلك الأجزاء كانت متباينة متفاصلة، وعلى هذا التقدير ففيه أيضاً احتمالان: أحدهما: أن يقال: إن تلك الأجزاء كانت متباينة، وكانت واقفة ساكنة من الأزل إلى الأبد، ثم إنها تحركت وامتزجت، وحدث من امتزاجها هذا العالم. وأول التوراة مشعر بهذا، لأنه قال: إنما حدث العالم، لأن ريح الله هبت في الظلمات، فتلك الأجزاء الواقفة لا شك أنها كانت في الظلمات، لأن عدم النور، فلما حركها الله تعالى كان ذلك عبارة عن قوله: الظلمة وريح الله هبت عليها.
When you know these details, we say: Those who said that the first matter is body and claimed that bodies are similar in complete essence—these have two sayings. The first: that bodies existed in eternity, and were devoid of all attributes, then attributes occurred in them. On this estimation, there are also two possibilities. One of them: that it is said that the whole was one connected body, then some parts separated from others in what continues [to happen], and this is what the saying of the Exalted alludes to: 'Have not those who disbelieved seen that the heavens and the earth were joined together, and We split them asunder?' [Quran 21:30]. And the second: that it is said that those parts were distinct and separate. On this estimation, there are also two possibilities. One of them: that it is said that those parts were distinct, and were stationary, motionless from eternity to eternity, then they moved and mixed, and from their mixing this world occurred. The beginning of the Torah alludes to this, because it said: 'The world occurred only because the wind of God blew in the darkness.' Those stationary parts were undoubtedly in darkness because of the absence of light. So when God, exalted, moved them, that was expressed by His saying: 'The darkness and the wind of God blew upon them.'
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage presents the opinions of those who hold that the first matter is body and that bodies are identical in essence. Such thinkers have two main sayings. The first saying is that bodies were eternal and devoid of all attributes, and then attributes occurred. Within this first saying, there are two possibilities: (1) All was one connected body that later split apart, which the author links to the Quranic verse (21:30) 'the heavens and the earth were joined together and We split them asunder.' (2) The parts were separate and distinct from the beginning. Then on this second possibility, there are two further possibilities: (a) Those separate parts were stationary from eternity and then moved and mixed, producing the world—this is alluded to in the beginning of the Torah, which speaks of darkness and the wind of God moving. (b) The parts were moving from eternity (which leads to the second saying, described in the next lesson). Thus, this lesson covers the first saying and its sub-divisions, including the Quranic and Torah references.

Misconception: The author endorses the Torah as a valid scripture.
Correction: The author only cites the Torah as an allusion used by proponents of this view, not as an authoritative source.

Checkpoint

A) It was separate parts stationary
B) It was one connected body
C) It was moving from eternity

Key Takeaway
The first saying on prime matter as body holds that eternal, attribute-less bodies eventually acquired attributes either through division of a single mass or through motion of separate parts, as argued from revelation.

Razi's Conclusion on Conflicting Views
Razi comments that the opinions are contradictory but each plausible, concluding the first branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's methodological stance on conflicting theological opinions and how he concludes the discussion of the first opinion on the identity of bodies.

Core Idea
Contradictory opinions can each be plausible – Razi shows scholarly humility.

In this brief but significant passage, Razi steps back from the competing interpretations we have examined. He does not choose one side but acknowledges that all are mutually contradictory yet each is internally possible. This reflects a mature scholarly approach: recognizing the limits of certainty when evidence is inconclusive. This passage also marks the conclusion of a major sub-discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully: 'واعلم أن هذه الأقوال متدافعة' – note the key words متدافعة (contradictory) and محتمل (possible).
2
Understand Razi's stance
Razi does not say which view is correct. He only observes that they conflict but each has internal plausibility. Why would he say this?
Think about scholarly responsibility when multiple interpretations exist.
3
See the conclusion
The phrase 'فهذا تمام الكلام' signals the end of the discussion on the branch stemming from the first opinion. This helps you track the structure of the larger argument.
This is a structural marker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الأقوال متدافعة ، وكل واحد منها فهو في نفسه محتمل . فهذا تمام الكلام في التفريع على قولنا : الأجسام بأسرها متماثلة
And know that these statements are contradictory, and each one of them is in itself possible. So this is the complete discussion on the branch of our statement: all bodies are identical.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is the author's evaluative comment on the preceding subgroups. Razi states that the various opinions on the origin of the elements are mutually contradictory. However, each one is inherently plausible. With this note, he concludes the discussion of the branch originating from the first position, namely that all bodies are identical in essence.

Correction: Razi only notes they are plausible but contradictory, not endorsing any.

B) They are all true

Key Takeaway
Razi acknowledges that conflicting opinions can each be internally plausible, and he concludes the sub-discussion on the identity of bodies.

The Hyle and Form Theory: Body as Composite of Hyle and Form
First aspect of second opinion: hyle and form theory with two groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hyle and form theory as presented in the second opinion, its explanation of body composition, and the two philosophical schools regarding the nature of hyle.

Core Idea
Hyle is the substrate without spatiality; corporeality inheres in it.

This passage presents the first sub-view of the second major opinion on primary matter. The author explains that body is composed of hyle (matter) and form, where hyle is a non-spatial substrate that receives corporeality. Two groups differ on whether hyle is existent and self-subsistent or non-existent. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second opinion context
The passage begins by stating the second opinion: primary matter is not body but something else. Read carefully to see how it relates to the first opinion.
2
Analyze the hyle and form composition
The author explains that body is composed of hyle and form. Note that hyle has no spatiality or volume, and corporeality inheres in it.
3
Distinguish the two groups
The proponents are divided: one group says hyle is existent and self-subsistent (al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, Plato, Aristotle), the other says hyle is non-existent. Understand the implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول الثاني : قول القائلين بأن المادة الأولى ليست هي الجسم ، بل المادة الأولى شيء آخر تحدث الجسمية فيه . وذلك الشيء هو المادة الأولى . والقائلون بهذا القول اختلفوا على وجوه :
فالوجه الأول : إن ذات الجسم مركبة من الهيولي والصورة . ومعنى هذا الكلام : أن الجسمية والحجمية ليست ذاتاً قائمة بنفسها ، وإنما هي حالة في محل ، وذلك المحل إذا اعتبرناه من حيث هو كان موجوداً ، ليس له حجمية ولا تحيز ، وكان مجرداً عن الحيز والجهة ، فإذا حلت الجسمية فيه ، حصل الجسم المتحيز . والقائلون بهذا القول فريقان : منهم من قال : إن ذلك المحل موجود قائم بنفسه ، ليس له تحيز ولا حجمية ولا حصول في الحيز ، وهذا هو قول أبي نصر الفارابي ، وأبي علي ابن سينا ، وهو مذهب أفلاطون وأرسطاطاليس ، ثم إن هذه الهيولى عند أفلاطون كانت خالية عن الجسمية والحجمية ، وهي قديمة أزلية . وأما الجسمية والتحيز فصفة حادثة ، ولها أول ومنهم من قال : إن تلك الهيولى ذوات معدومة ، وهذا هو قول القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء . وبالله التوفيق
The second opinion: the opinion of those who say that the primary matter is not body, but the primary matter is something else in which corporeality arises. That thing is the primary matter. Those who hold this opinion differed in aspects. The first aspect: that the essence of body is composed of hyle and form. The meaning of this speech: corporeality and spatiality are not an essence subsisting by itself; rather they are a state in a substrate. That substrate, when we consider it insofar as it is existent, has no spatiality nor occupation of space, and is abstracted from place and direction. When corporeality inheres in it, the spatial body is obtained. Those who hold this opinion are two groups: among them are those who say that that substrate is existent, subsisting by itself, with no occupation of space, no spatiality, and no occurrence in place. This is the saying of Abu Nasr al-Farabi and Abu Ali Ibn Sina, and it is the doctrine of Plato and Aristotle. Then this hyle according to Plato was devoid of corporeality and spatiality, and it is eternal and pre-eternal. As for corporeality and spatiality, they are an originated attribute with a beginning. And among them are those who say that that hyle are non-existent entities, and this is the saying of those who say that the non-existent is a thing. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major opinion on primary matter: it is not body but something else in which corporeality occurs. Within this opinion, the first aspect is the theory of hyle and form. The author explains that corporeality and spatiality are not self-subsistent; they inhere in a substrate called hyle. The hyle, considered in itself, has no spatiality or extension and is abstracted from place and direction. When corporeality inheres in it, a spatially extended body results. Proponents of this view are divided into two groups: one holds that the hyle is an existent, self-subsistent entity without spatiality, attributed to al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, Plato, and Aristotle. According to Plato, this hyle is eternal and devoid of corporeality, while corporeality and spatiality are originated attributes. The other group holds that the hyle are non-existent entities, consistent with the doctrine that 'the non-existent is a thing.' The author closes with 'And with God is success.'

Key Terms
هيولى (hayūlā)
صورة (ṣūrah)
تحيز (taḥayyuz)
محل (maḥall)
مادة أولى (māddah ūlā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hyle theory denies creation of the world.
Correction: The hyle theory does not necessarily deny creation; it explains the composition of bodies within a philosophical framework. For example, Plato holds that hyle is eternal but corporeality is originated.

Checkpoint
According to the hyle and form theory, what is the nature of corporeality (jismiyyah)?
A) It is a self-subsistent essence.
B) It is an accident inhering in a substrate.
C) It is eternal and pre-eternal.
D) It is non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The hyle and form theory explains that body is composed of a non-spatial substrate (hyle) and the form of corporeality, with disagreement on whether hyle is existent or non-existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes the hyle as having no spatiality or extension. Do you ever consider that the physical world you perceive is composed of entities that are not themselves physical? How does this affect your view of the material world as the ultimate reality?

Plato believed the hyle is eternal, while corporeality is originated. What does this imply about the relationship between the eternal and the temporal? Are you comfortable with the idea that there may be eternal entities besides God?
?
The second group says the hyle is non-existent, based on the doctrine that 'the non-existent is a thing.' How do you reconcile the idea that something that does not exist can still be considered a 'thing'? Does this affect your understanding of God's creation from nothing?

Objection and First Answer: Impossibilities Without Essences
The objector argues the proof assumes essences in nonexistence; the first answer uses universal impossibilities as counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first answer to the objection defends the eternity of impossibility without assuming that non-existents are things.

Core Idea
Impossibilities can be eternal without being positive entities; they are pure negations.

In this passage, an objector challenges the first proof by claiming it only works if non-existents have fixed essences, a view the author rejects. The first answer counters by pointing to universally accepted impossibilities that are eternal yet are not considered things. This lesson unpacks that response and its key examples, showing how impossibilities can be recognized without granting essences to non-existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objector's claim: the proof only holds if essences exist in both states (existence and non-existence). The objector denies that non-existents are things, so the proof doesn't apply.
Notice the logical structure: 'if it is said... we say...'
2
Examine the first answer and its examples
The answer gives two examples: impossibility of combining opposites, and impossibility of one being double of two. These are universally recognized as impossible in themselves, and their impossibility is eternal.
The answer emphasizes that no one considers these impossibilities as 'things' or essences.
3
Understand the final analogy
The answer concludes: if the world were ever essentially impossible, its impossibility would be eternal, just like the examples—even without assuming non-existents are things. This shows the objection fails.
Think about how the analogy uses the same logic as the examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا الكلام إنما يتم إذا قلنا : الماهيات مفتقرة حالتي الوجود والعدم، حتى يقال : إن تلك الماهية لما اقتضت أمراً، وجب بقاء ذلك الإقتضاء أبداً . وهذا بناء على أن المعدوم شيء، ونحن لا نقول به، فلا يلزمنا هذا الكلام . فنقول : . الجواب عنه من وجوه : الأول : إن جميع العقلاء يقولون : الجمع بين الضدين ممتنع لعينه ولذاته ، فلا جرم وجب بقاء هذا الامتناع أبداً ، وأن كون الواحد ضعف الاثنين ممتنع لذاته ، فوجب بقاء هذا الامتناع أبداً . ثم إن ثم إن أحداً من العقلاء لم يقل : إن الممتنعات أشياء في العدم، بل هي عـدمات محضة وسلوب صرفة، وليس لها ماهيات ولا أعيان . فإذا عقل هذا، فلم لا يعقل أيضاً ههنا أن نقول : لو صدق على العالم في وقت من الأوقات، أنه ممتنع لعينه ولذاته ، لوجب أن يبقى هذا الامتناع أبداً ، وإن كنا لا نقول : بأن المعدوم شيء ؟
If it is said: This speech is only valid if we say that essences are in need of both states of existence and non-existence, so that it can be said that when that essence required something, that requirement must remain forever. And this is based on the assumption that the non-existent is a thing, and we do not say that, so this speech does not apply to us. Then we say: The answer to it is in several ways: The first: All rational people say that the conjunction of opposites is impossible in its very essence and in itself, so inevitably this impossibility must remain forever; and that one being double of two is impossible in itself, so this impossibility must remain forever. Moreover, not one of the rational people said that impossibilities are things in non-existence; rather, they are pure non-existences and sheer negations, and they have no essences nor identities. So if this is understood, then why is it not also understood here that we can say: If it were true of the world at some time that it is impossible in its very essence and in itself, then this impossibility would have to remain forever, even if we do not say that the non-existent is a thing?
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the first proof and its first answer. The objector argues that the proof only works if we assume that essences exist in both states of existence and non-existence (i.e., that a non-existent thing has a fixed essence). Since the author (presumably a mutakallim who denies the existence of non-existents as things) does not hold that, the proof does not apply. The first answer responds by pointing out that universally accepted impossibilities—like the impossibility of combining opposites or one being double of two—are acknowledged as eternal impossibilities even though they are not things in non-existence. Rational people agree that these impossibilities are not positive entities; they are pure non-existences and negations, without essences or identities. By analogy, if the world were ever essentially impossible, that impossibility would likewise be eternal, regardless of whether one considers non-existents as things. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
ماهية
ممتنع
عدم
سلوب
محضة
جمع الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first answer implies that impossibilities are positive things that exist in non-existence.
Correction: The answer explicitly states that impossibilities are pure non-existences and sheer negations, not things with essences or identities.

Checkpoint
What example does the first answer give to show that impossibility can be eternal without assuming non-existents have essences?
A) The impossibility of the world being eternal.
B) The impossibility of combining opposites.
C) The impossibility of the power of God.
D) The impossibility of the world having no beginning.

Key Takeaway
The first answer demonstrates that impossibilities can be eternal without being positive entities, thus defending the proof against the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that the conjunction of opposites is impossible in itself—an eternal truth no one disputes. But in your own life, you often claim certain bad habits or sins are 'impossible to give up'. Are you treating a mere difficulty as an essential impossibility, and using that as an excuse to remain unchanged? What would it mean for you to confront that distinction today?
?
The objector assumed that the proof requires non-existents to be 'things'. You too might assume that the only way God's power is limited is by some positive entity. But this lesson teaches that impossibilities are pure negations (عدمات محضة). Is your fear of certain future trials rooted in a belief that they are positive forces, rather than mere absences of Allah's grace? How does seeing them as negations change your trust in Him?

Positive vs. Negative Attributes in Kalam
Sanusi distinguishes positive and negative attributes to refute an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distinguishing positive and negative attributes resolves an objection about describing non-existents.

Core Idea
Not all attributes are positive; negative attributes can describe without implying real existence.

In this lesson, we explore the second answer to an objection against a proof about the world's creation. The objector claimed that describing non-existence with impossibility or possibility would make non-existence a 'thing.' Sanusi's response hinges on a crucial distinction: are impossibility and possibility positive or negative attributes? Let’s see how this distinction defuses the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: 'وصف وجودي' and 'وصف عدمي'.
2
Understand the objection being answered
The objection claimed that because the first proof described the world before existence with impossibility, it implied non-existents are real things. Identify this background.
3
Grasp the core distinction
The answer says: if impossibility and possibility were positive attributes, the objection would stand. But they are negative attributes, so no problem.
4
Test with the example given
Sanusi gives a proof: attributing negative predicates to negative subjects is perfectly valid (e.g., 'non-existence is not existing'). This shows the objection fails.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this distinction applies to other theological discussions. Why does it matter that attributes can be positive or negative?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني : إن وصف العدم بالامتناع والإمكان ، إنما يوجب القول بأن المعدوم شيء ، لو ثبت أن الامتناع أو الإمكان وصفان وجوديان، أما إذا كانا وصفين عدميين، لم يلزم من إسنادهما إلى الماهية قبل وجودها ، كون تلك الماهية ذاتاً وعيناً بدليل : أن إسناد المحمولات العدمية إلى المـوضـوعــات العدمية : غير ممتنع البتة أصلاً .
And the second way: That describing non-existence with impossibility and possibility only necessitates the statement that the non-existent is a thing if it is established that impossibility or possibility are positive attributes. But if they are negative attributes, then it does not follow from their attribution to the essence before its existence that that essence is an entity and an identity, as evidence: The attribution of negative predicates to negative subjects is not impossible at all, absolutely.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage provides the second answer to the objection. The objector claimed that the first proof requires treating non-existents as things because it ascribes impossibility to the world before its existence. The second answer responds by making a key distinction: impossibility and possibility are not positive attributes (وجوديان) but negative ones (عدمیان). If they were positive, then describing non-existence would require the non-existent to be a thing. But since they are negative, their predication does not imply that the subject is a positive entity. As a proof, the author notes that attributing negative predicates (like 'not existing') to negative subjects (like 'non-existence itself') is perfectly valid and does not require the subject to be a real thing. Therefore, the objection based on the assumption that non-existents are things is irrelevant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: All attributes are positive and imply real existence of the subject.
Correction: Negative attributes (such as impossibility and possibility in this context) do not imply the subject's real existence. They can be attributed to non-existents without making them things.

Impossibility and possibility are negative attributes; describing non-existence with them does not make the non-existent a real thing.

The Question That Never Goes Away
The sixth argument refutes the question as self-undermining.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the sixth argument refutes the question of God's will attaching to a specific time by showing its universal recurrence.

Core Idea
A question that never goes away is no question at all.

This lesson presents a clever logical refutation. Instead of answering why God's will attached to this time, the argument turns the question back on itself: the same question could be asked for any time, so it is inherently flawed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Let the argument sink in without analysis.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the conditional: if the will attached to another time, the question would recur. Then the conclusion: since it recurs for all times, it is invalid.
This is a reductio ad absurdum style argument.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Ask yourself: does this argument successfully show the question is flawed? Why does recurrence make a question invalid?
The question assumes a specific time is special, but the argument shows no time is special.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس في الجواب: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن قول القائل: لم تعلقت إرادة الله تعالى بإحداث العالم في هذا الوقت؟ هو سؤال باطل، لأن تلك الإرادة لو تعلقت بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر، لا في هذا الوقت، لكان ذلك السؤال المذكور عائداً، ولما كان ذلك السؤال عائداً على كل التقديرات كان باطلاً.
The sixth aspect of the answer: Why is it not permissible to say: The statement of the one who says, 'Why did God's will attach to bringing about the world at this time?' is an invalid question, because if that will had attached to bringing about the world at another time, not this time, then that same question would recur; and since that question would recur in all estimations, it is invalid.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is the sixth argument, which claims that the question itself is invalid because it could be asked of any time. If the world were created at a different time, the same question would arise. Thus, the question is not specific to this time and is in principle unanswerable since it applies equally to all times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: This argument shows that the question is not valid because it can be asked of any time, making it a general complaint rather than a specific inquiry. It does not depend on the actual time, so it is logically redundant.

The sixth argument refutes the question by showing it would recur for all times, rendering it invalid.

Distinction between al-Mujib and al-Qadir
The author answers the second question by distinguishing the necessitating cause from the powerful agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental difference between the necessitating cause (al-Mujib) and the powerful agent (al-Qadir), focusing on the requirement of distinct essences for choice-based action.

Core Idea
The power to choose between alternatives requires prior distinct essences.

In this passage, the author clarifies a critical distinction in understanding divine action. He explains that a powerful agent (al-Qadir) can bring about one thing instead of another, which implies that the essences of possible things must be distinct before they exist. In contrast, a necessitating cause (al-Mujib) produces its effect without needing such prior distinction. This distinction helps resolve a key theological question about God's power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the technical terms 'الموجب' and 'القادر'.
The author answers a second question here, so this builds on earlier material.
2
Identify the definition of al-Qadir
The author defines al-Qadir as one from whom it is valid to bring into existence this instead of that. Understand that this implies choice.
3
Understand the requirement for distinct essences
The author argues that for choice to be meaningful, the essences of possible things must be distinct before they exist. Otherwise, saying 'this instead of that' would be meaningless.
This is the key logical point.
4
Contrast with al-Mujib
Al-Mujib does not require such prior distinct essences; it simply causes its effect necessarily.
Contrast highlights the uniqueness of al-Qadir.
5
Apply the distinction to divine attributes
Reflect on how this distinction helps in understanding God as al-Qadir – able to choose among alternatives, not a mere necessitating cause.
This connects to broader theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن السؤال الثاني: إن الفرق بين الموجب والقادر: أن القادر هو الذي يصح منه إيجاد هذا بدلاً عن ذاك وبالضد. فلولا امتياز بعض هذه الماهيات عن بعض قبل دخولها في الوجود، وإلا لما صح أن يقال: إنه يقدر على إيجاد هذا بدلاً عن ذاك، فهذا يقتضي أن يكون تعين تلك الماهيات متقدماً على تعلق القدرة بها. ولما كان المقدور أثراً لتلك القدرة، لزم كونها متأخرة عن تلك القدرة. أما الموجب فإنه لا تأثير له إلا وقع الأثر به، ولا يقتضي أن يكون تعيين تلك الماهيات سابقاً على كونه مقتضياً لها. فظهر الفرق.
The answer to the second question: Indeed the difference between al-mujib (the necessitating cause) and al-qadir (the powerful agent) is that al-qadir is the one from whom it is valid to bring into existence this instead of that, and the opposite. If there were no distinction of some of these essences from others before their entry into existence, it would not be valid to say: He is able to bring into existence this instead of that. So this necessitates that the determination of those essences be prior to the attachment of power to them. And since the object of power is an effect of that power, it follows that it is posterior to that power. As for al-mujib, it has no effect except that the effect occurs by it, and it does not require that the determination of those essences be prior to its being a necessitating cause for them. So the difference appears.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the second question, defining the distinction between al-mujib (the necessitating cause) and al-qadir (the powerful agent). The author defines al-qadir as one who can validly produce this instead of that and its opposite. He then explains that this ability requires that the essences (al-mahiyat) be distinct from each other before they come into existence; otherwise, it would not be correct to say he can produce this instead of that. Therefore, the determination of those essences must precede the attachment of power to them. Since the object of power (al-maqdur) is an effect of that power, it follows that the power must be prior to the effect. In contrast, al-mujib has no such requirement: it simply causes its effect without needing a prior distinction of essences. Thus the difference is clear: al-qadir acts with choice among alternatives, while al-mujib acts necessarily.

Correction: The passage clarifies that the key distinction is that al-Qadir can bring into existence this instead of that, which implies choice, whereas al-Mujib produces its effect necessarily without requiring prior distinct essences.
