Two Proofs Distinguishing Resolve from Intention
Two proofs showing al-azm (resolve) is distinct from al-qasd (intention).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments that prove prior resolve (al-azm) is not identical to actual intention (al-qasd), and how this refutes the claim of an eternal will.

Core Idea
Resolve and intention differ in essence because their effects and concomitants are mutually exclusive.

In this passage, the author presents two clear proofs to show that a prior resolve to act in the future is not the same as the intention to act when the time arrives. The first proof examines the role of knowledge of time, while the second looks at the quiddities and their necessary concomitants. These arguments not only establish a conceptual distinction but also refute a theological claim about the eternity of the will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first proof (conditional reasoning), second proof (quiddity and concomitants), and a concluding refutation of the eternity claim.
2
Analyze the first proof
The author uses a conditional: if resolve and intention were identical, then a person with resolve would intend even without knowing the time. Since this is not the case, they are distinct. Understand why knowledge of time is crucial here.
3
Analyze the second proof
Resolve and intention have different quiddities because their necessary concomitants – leaving the act vs. doing it in present/future – are mutually exclusive. The difference in concomitants proves the difference in essences.
4
Understand the refutation of the eternity claim
The passage concludes that the distinction between resolve and intention implies that the will under discussion cannot be eternal. This refutes the false claim that this will is eternal.
Note the final sentence: 'Thus this is to show that the statement of the eternity of that will is false.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمجرد تلك الإرادة الأولى لا يصير قاصداً لإيقاع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت. ولو كان العزم على أن يفعل الفعل الفلاني في الوقت المستقبل، هو نفس القصد إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل عند حضور ذلك الوقت، لوجب في هذه الصورة التي ذكرناها: أن قاصداً إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل، عند حضور ذلك الوقت، سواء علم أن ذلك الوقت حضر، أو لم يعلم ذلك. ولما لم يكن الأمر كذلك، علمنا: أن العزم على الفعل [الفلاني] في الوقت المعين، من الأوقات المستقبلية: ليس هو [نفس] القصد إلى إيقاع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت بل الحق أنه إذا عزم [على] أن يفعل الفعل الفلاني، في وقت معين من الأوقات المستقبلة، ثم استمر ذلك العزم [الأول] إلى أن حضر ذلك الوقت، وعلم ذلك العازم، حضور ذلك الوقت. فإن بقاء العزم [الأول] مع العلم بحضور ذلك الوقت: يوجبان حدوث قصد متعلق، بإيقاع ذلك الفعل. فيثبت: أن العزم المتقدم، لا يكفي في إيقاع الفعل في الوقت الذي سيحضر بل لا بد من حدوث قصد متعلق بإيقاع ذلك الفعل.

الثاني: إن العزم على الإيجاد في الزمان المستقبل: له ماهية. والقصد إلى إيجاد الفعل في الحال: له ماهية أخرى. إحدى الماهيتين لا تساوي الأخرى بدليل أن كل واحدة من هاتين الماهيتين، لا تقوم مقام الأخرى. فإن العزم على الإيجاد في المستقبل، يوجب تركه في الحال. وفعله في المستقبل. والقصد إلى الفعل في الحال، يوجب الفعل في الحال، والترك في المستقبل فهذان النوعان من الإرادة ولوازمها متنافية. واختلاف اللوازم، يدل على اختلاف [ماهيات] الملزومات. وإذا ثبت هذا، كان القول بأن العزم على الإيجاد في المستقبل، يصير غير القصد إلى الإيجاد في الحال: معناه: أن حقيقة مخصوصة، تصير [هي] بعينها عين حقيقة أخرى، تخالفها في الماهية والنوعية. وذلك محال. فهذا لبيان أن القول بقدوم تلك الإرادة: قول باطل.
By merely that first will, he does not become intending to perform that action at that time. And if the resolve to do a certain action in the future were identical to the intention to perform that action when that time arrives, then in the scenario we mentioned, it would necessitate that he be intending to perform that action when that time arrives, whether he knows that the time has arrived or does not know it. Since it is not the case, we know that the resolve to do [a certain] action at a specific time among future times is not [identical to] the intention to perform that action at that time. Rather, the truth is that when he resolves [to] do a certain action at a specific time among future times, then that resolve [first] continues until that time arrives, and that resolver knows that the time has arrived. For the persistence of the [first] resolve together with the knowledge of the arrival of that time necessitates the occurrence of an intention directed to performing that action. Thus it is established that the prior resolve is not sufficient for performing the action at the time that will arrive; rather, the occurrence of an intention directed to performing that action is necessary.

Second: The resolve to bring about existence in the future has a quiddity. And the intention to bring about the action in the present has another quiddity. One of the two quiddities is not equal to the other, as evidenced by the fact that each of these two quiddities does not stand in the place of the other. For the resolve to bring about existence in the future necessitates leaving it in the present and doing it in the future. And the intention to do the action in the present necessitates doing it in the present and leaving it in the future. So these two types of will and their concomitants are mutually exclusive. The difference of concomitants indicates the difference of the [quiddities] of the substantives. And if this is established, then the statement that the resolve to bring about existence in the future becomes other than the intention to bring about existence in the present means that a particular essence becomes itself precisely another essence that differs from it in quiddity and type. And that is impossible. Thus this is to show that the statement of the eternity of that will is false.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs to distinguish between prior resolve (al-azm) and actual intention (al-qasd), and then concludes that the will under discussion cannot be eternal. The author first sets up a conditional: if resolve and intention were identical, then a person who resolves to do an action at a future time would necessarily intend to do it when that time arrives, even if he does not know the time has arrived. Since this is not the case (a person may have a resolve but not yet intend upon waking), they are different. The correct view is that persistence of the resolve together with knowledge of the time's arrival causes a new intention to arise. Thus prior resolve alone is insufficient; a new intention is needed.

The second proof examines the quiddities (mahiyat) of the two. Resolve to do something in the future requires leaving it in the present and doing it in the future. Intention to do something now requires doing it now and leaving it in the future. Their concomitants differ, so the quiddities differ. If one claimed they become identical, that would mean one essence becomes another different essence—an impossibility.

The author then uses this to refute the claim that the will in question is eternal (qadim): the above reasoning shows that such a will cannot be eternal, leading to the conclusion that the statement of its eternity is false.

Key Terms
العزم (al-azm: resolve)
القصد (al-qasd: intention)
الإرادة (al-irada: will)
الماهية (al-mahiya: quiddity/essence)
اللوازم (al-lawazim: concomitants)
القدم (al-qidam: eternity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Resolve (al-azm) and intention (al-qasd) are the same thing.
Correction: This passage shows they are distinct: resolve is a prior determination that does not automatically produce action, while intention is the immediate inclination that arises with knowledge of the time and necessitates action accordingly.

Checkpoint

B) The difference in their quiddities and concomitant effects
C) The fact that one is voluntary and the other involuntary
D) The impossibility of an infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Resolve and intention are distinct in quiddity and effect, and the will under discussion cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have resolved to pray Fajr on time every day, but when the alarm goes off, you often hit snooze. Is your resolve identical to intention? If not, what is missing in your heart that prevents the intention from arising? This lesson forces you to ask: are you just a resolver, or are you an intender? What concrete step will you take tonight to ensure your resolve becomes true intention at Fajr?
?
The second proof shows that resolve and intention have mutually exclusive concomitants: one leads to leaving now, the other to acting now. In your life right now, which 'type of will' dominates—are you always 'resolving' to change later, or are you actually 'intending' to act now? Where are you deceiving yourself by thinking resolve is enough? Write down one specific area where you will stop resolving and start intending, starting now.
?
If it is impossible for one essence to become another, then your character cannot change by mere resolve alone—you need a new intention created by Allah at each moment. Have you been relying on your own resolve to reform yourself, rather than begging Allah to create in you a new intention to obey Him? What does this passage teach you about your absolute dependence on His creation of your will? Call upon Him right now to grant you the intention to act.

Why God's Will Cannot Be Temporal
A proof of the impossibility of a temporal will with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a temporal will leads to an infinite regress and how the objection from daily events is addressed.

Core Idea
A temporal will would require an infinite chain of wills to specify its time, which is impossible.

Kalam often argues that God's will must be eternal, not created in time. This passage presents a classic proof: if the will were temporal, its occurrence at a specific moment would need another will to determine it, leading to an infinite regress. The author then addresses the natural objection: what about the daily events we observe that seem to involve temporal wills? Read on to see how the argument is defended.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting assumption
Read the passage once through, noting that the author begins by assuming the will is temporal. Identify why this assumption is problematic.
The key term here is 'haditha' (temporal/created).
2
Follow the infinite regress reasoning
Trace the logic: a temporal will occurs at a specific time, but it could have occurred at another time. That specification requires another will, and so on. See how this leads to an impossibility.
The term 'tasalsul' means infinite regress, which is considered impossible in kalam.
3
Examine the objection and its answer
The opponent brings up daily events that involve temporal wills. Notice how the author responds: the objection applies to all arguments, but the answer is already given in the first method.
The phrase 'wa Allah a'lam' indicates humility before divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا: إنه يمتنع كونها حادثة، وذلك لأنها لما كانت حادثة، كان حدوثها بوقت [معين] مع جواز حصول الحدوث قبله أو بعده. فوجب أن لا يحصل ذلك الاختصاص إلا لإرادة أخرى. والكلام فيها كما في الأول؛ فيلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. فيثبت: أنه لو حصلت تلك الإرادة [لكانت] إما قديمة أو حادثة، وثبت فساد القسمين، فلزم الحكم بامتناع حصول الإرادة. لا يقال هذا معارض بالحوادث اليومية. لأنا نقول: المعارضة بالحوادث اليومية واردة على كل هذه الوجوه والجواب الممكن: ما ذكرناه في الطريقة الأولى [والله أعلم].
And we only said: It is impossible for it to be temporal, because if it were temporal, its occurrence would be at a specific time, while it is possible for it to occur before or after. So it would be necessary that that specification does not occur except through another will. And the discussion regarding it is as in the first; thus an infinite regress follows, which is impossible. So it is established that if that will occurred, it would be either eternal or temporal, and the falsity of both divisions is established, so it is necessary to judge the impossibility of the occurrence of will. One should not say: This is rebutted by daily events. For we say: The rebuttal by daily events applies to all these ways. And the possible answer is what we mentioned in the first method. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage argues that a temporal will (al-irada al-haditha) is impossible. If the will were temporal, it would occur at a specific time even though it could have occurred earlier or later. This specification (ikhtisas) to a particular moment requires another will to determine it. That second will faces the same issue, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Thus the will cannot be temporal. Combined with the previous refutation of its eternity, both possibilities are ruled out, so the will cannot occur at all. An objection is raised: this reasoning seems contradicted by daily events (hawadith yawmiyya) where temporal wills occur. The author responds that the objection applies to all these arguments, and the possible answer is the one given in the first method (likely referring to a prior argument not fully quoted here). The phrase 'And Allah knows best' indicates humility before divine knowledge.

Key Terms
حادثة
اختصاص
تسلسل
حوادث يومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that daily events, such as human decisions, provide clear examples of temporal wills that contradict the argument.
Correction: The argument is about the divine will, not human will. The author says the objection is general and has been answered in the first method, implying that even daily events ultimately trace back to an eternal cause.

Checkpoint
What problem does the argument identify if the will were temporal (حادثة)?
A) It would be impossible to know the time of occurrence
B) It would require another will to specify its time, leading to infinite regress
C) It would contradict the eternity of God
D) It would make the will changeable

Key Takeaway
God's will cannot be temporal because that would require an infinite chain of wills to determine its specific time, and the apparent temporal wills in daily events are answered by a prior argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If God's will is eternal and unchanging, are you truly at peace with His decree? Or do you secretly wish your own temporal will could override His? What would it take for you to surrender fully?

The Eternal Will's Relation is a Necessary Concomitant
Division and refutation of the non-necessity option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the relation of the eternal will to the specific time of creation must be a necessary concomitant of its quiddity, and why the alternative leads to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
The eternal will's specific relation either is necessary or needs a cause—the latter leads to absurdity.

In this passage, al-Sanusi presents a second argument concerning the eternal will. He begins by dividing the relation of the will to a specific time into two possibilities: either it is a necessary concomitant of the will's essence, or it is not. The second option is shown to be impossible because it would require an external cause, leading to an infinite regress. This argument is foundational for understanding how God's will relates to creation without change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the logical division.
The passage uses a classic 'either/or' structure.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The first is that the relation is a necessary concomitant; the second is that it is not. Understand what each means.
3
Understand the refutation
The second option is false because it would require an external cause for the relation, leading to an infinite regress.
Infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible in kalam.
4
Conclude the first part
Since the second option is invalid, the relation must be a necessary concomitant.
This conclusion sets up the next part about persistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: تعلق الإرادة القديمة بإيقاع العالم في ذلك الوقت المخصوص المعين، إما أن يكون من لوازم ماهية تلك الإرادة وإما أن لا يكون. والثاني باطل، وإلا لافتقرت تلك الإرادة في حصول ذلك التعلق المخصوص، إلى سبب آخر، ولزم التسلسل. ولما بطل هذا القسم، بقي الحق هو الأول. فنقول: إذا دخل العالم في الوجود في ذلك الوقت المعين، فبعد انقضاء ذلك الوقت [المعين] وبعد دخول العالم في الوجود يمتنع أن تبقى الإرادة متعلقة بإحداث العالم في ذلك الوقت المعين. ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إنه بعد دخوله في الوجود، يمتنع إدخاله في الوجود. لأن تكوين الكائن وإيجاد [الموجد] محال.
The second argument: We say: The relation of the eternal will to bringing the world into existence at that specific particular time is either a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of that will or not. The second is false, because then that will would need another cause for that specific relation, and an infinite regress would follow. Since this division is invalid, the truth remains the first. So we say: When the world comes into existence at that specific time, then after the passing of that [specific] time and after the coming of the world into existence, it is impossible for the will to remain related to bringing the world into existence at that specific time. Two proofs indicate this: First: After it comes into existence, bringing it into existence is impossible, because the formation of what is formed and the bringing into existence of [the existent] is impossible.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument concerning the eternal will (al-irada al-qadima). It begins with a division: the relation of the eternal will to bringing the world into existence at a specific time is either a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the will's quiddity or not. The second option is false because it would require an external cause for that relation, leading to an infinite regress. So the relation must be necessary (the first option). However, once the world exists at that specific time, after the time passes and the world exists, the will cannot remain related to bringing the world into existence at that time, because that would mean bringing into existence something that already exists—an impossibility. Two proofs are promised; the first is given: after the world comes into existence, bringing it into existence again is impossible, as forming what is already formed is impossible. The second proof is not included in this window.

Key Terms
الإرادة القديمة (al-irada al-qadima)
تعلق (ta'alluq)
لازم (lazim)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eternal will's relation to a specific time is arbitrary and could change.
Correction: The argument shows it must be a necessary concomitant, otherwise infinite regress.

Checkpoint
Why must the relation of the eternal will to the specific time be a necessary concomitant?
A) Because God is all-powerful
B) Otherwise it would need a cause, leading to infinite regress
C) Because the time is specific
D) Because the will is eternal

Key Takeaway
The eternal will's specific relation to the time of creation is a necessary concomitant of its quiddity, not an accidental addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will is perfectly wise and not subject to external influences? Does your daily life reflect trust in His perfect wisdom, especially when His timing doesn't match your expectations?
?
When you face delays or unanswered prayers, do you recognize that God's timing is intrinsically linked to His will, not arbitrary? How can you cultivate deeper patience in light of this understanding?
?
How does the logical certainty that God's will is self-sufficient and unchanging affect your reliance on Him? Are there areas of your life where you act as if God's will might be contingent on something else?

The Will Cannot Remain Related After the Event
Proof that the will cannot remain related after the event, with one proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the eternal will cannot remain related to the creation of the world after the world has come into existence, based on the impossibility of creating what already exists.

Core Idea
After the world exists, the will cannot relate to creating it again—that would be impossible.

Having established that the relation is necessary, al-Sanusi now shows that this relation cannot persist after the world's creation. When the world comes into existence at the predetermined time, after that time passes, the will's attachment to bringing it into existence at that time becomes impossible. He provides two proofs; the first is given here: you cannot bring into existence something that already exists, as forming what is already formed is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the claim and the proof.
The passage builds on the previous lesson's conclusion.
2
Understand the claim
After the world exists at that time, the will cannot remain related to that act of creation.
3
Analyze the first proof
The proof: you cannot bring into existence what already exists. Forming what is already formed is impossible.
This is a self-evident logical principle.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows the will's relation is temporally bound to the moment of creation and does not persist.

Source Text

Commentary

Three Proofs of Distinction in Non-Existence
Three proofs that distinction occurs in pure non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three logical proofs that distinction can exist in pure non-existence and negation, and why this refutes the claim that distinction requires existence.

Core Idea
Non-existences can be really distinct by their logical relations and properties.

After establishing that known non-existences must be distinct, the author now faces the objection that distinction requires existence. In response, he provides three proofs showing that real distinctions can exist between pure non-existences. This lesson walks through these proofs in detail.

Guided Path
1
Context and the objection
Recall that the author has argued that known attributes must be distinct. The opponent objects that distinction requires existence. Read the opening lines of the passage to see how the author grants the need for distinction but challenges the requirement of existence.
The author says: 'We grant that distinction is necessary now. But why does that require existence?'
2
First proof: implicans and necessary condition
The first proof uses the logical relationship between an implicans (ملزوم) and its necessary condition (لازم). The non-existence of the implicans does not necessitate the non-existence of the necessary condition, but the converse is true. These two non-existences are distinguished by real properties.
Even though both are non-existences, they have different logical consequences.
3
Second proof: contraries in a substrate
The second proof considers contraries (أضداد). The non-existence of one contrary in a substrate makes the substrate suitable for the other contrary. This property is not present in the non-existence of a non-contrary, showing a real distinction between non-existences.
The effect on the substrate demonstrates the distinction.
4
Third proof: negation vs affirmation
The third proof concerns the judgment that two contradictories cannot both be absent. This judgment implies that negation (سلب) has a particularity and distinction from affirmation (إيجاب), otherwise the opposition would not be intelligible.
If negation had no distinct property, we could not say it is opposed to affirmation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : سلمنا أنه لا بد من كون تلك المعلومات متمايزة في الحال، فلم قلتم : إن حصول الامتياز في الحال يستدعي كونها موجودة؟ وما الدليل عليه؟ بل نقول : الدليل على أن الامتياز والاختلاف قد يحصل في العدم المحض والسلب الصرف : وجوه : الأول : إن عدم الملزوم لا يوجب عدم اللازم. لكن عدم اللازم يقتضي عدم الملزوم، فكل واحد من هذين العدمين ممتاز عن الآخر بخاصية حقيقية ونوعية ذاتية. الثاني : إن عدم الضد عن المحل يصحح حلول الضد الآخر في ذلك المحل، وعدم ما لا يكون ضداً لا يفيد هذا الحكم. الثالث : إنا إذا قلنا : النقيضان لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان، فقد حكمنا بكون السلب منافياً للإيجاب. فلو لم يكن للسلب من حيث هو سلب خصوصية وامتياز عن الإيجاب، وإلا لما صح الحكم بكونه منافياً للإيجاب.
Then we say: We grant that it is necessary for those known things to be distinct now. But why do you say that the occurrence of distinction now necessitates them being existent? And what is the evidence for it? Rather we say: The evidence that distinction and difference can occur in pure non-existence and pure negation are several aspects: First: The non-existence of the implicans does not necessitate the non-existence of the necessary condition. But the non-existence of the necessary condition necessitates the non-existence of the implicans. So each of these two non-existences is distinguished from the other by a real specific property and an essential type property. Second: The non-existence of a contrary in a substrate renders it suitable for the other contrary to settle, and the non-existence of what is not a contrary does not yield this judgment. Third: When we say: The two contradictories neither meet nor are both absent, we have judged that negation is opposed to affirmation. So if negation as such had no particularity and distinction from affirmation, the judgment of its being opposed to affirmation would not be sound.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the third response, which grants that distinction must be present now but argues that distinction does not require existence. The responder provides three proofs that distinction can occur in pure non-existence and pure negation. The first proof involves the logical relationship between the implicans and its necessary condition: their non-existences are distinguished by real properties. The second proof involves the non-existence of a contrary, which, unlike non-existence of a non-contrary, has an effect on the substrate's suitability. The third proof involves the distinction between negation and affirmation, showing that negation has a specific property that distinguishes it from affirmation, otherwise the judgment of opposition would not be sound.

Key Terms
عدم
امتياز
اختلاف
ملزوم
لازم
ضد
نقيضان
سلب
إيجاب
خاصية
نوعية ذاتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The responder is claiming that non-existence has positive existence or that non-existences are positively existing entities.
Correction: The responder is not attributing positive existence to non-existences. Rather, he argues that real distinctions can exist between non-existences based on their logical relations and effects, without their being existent.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof that distinction can occur in non-existence?
A) The non-existence of the necessary condition necessitates the non-existence of the implicans.
B) The non-existence of the implicans and the non-existence of the necessary condition are distinguished by real properties.
C) Negation is opposed to affirmation.
D) The non-existence of a contrary makes a substrate suitable for the other contrary.

Key Takeaway
Distinction does not require existence; logical relations can ground real distinctions even in non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If pure non-existences can be distinguished by their logical relations, what does that say about the nature of your own moral failures? Is your failure to pray just a blank nothingness, or does it carry a real distinction that affects your soul's state? How will you treat your sins as real absences that must be filled, not mere nothings to ignore?
?
The non-existence of one contrary makes a substrate ready for its opposite. In your heart, what 'contrary' are you missing that is making you vulnerable to its evil opposite? Are you aware that your lack of certain virtues is not neutral but actively prepares the ground for vices? What will you do today to install that virtue before the vice takes root?

Distinction of the Known as Self-Evident
defends the self-evident nature of the distinction of the known

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the premise that the known must be distinguished from other things is considered self-evident in Kalam.

Core Idea
Knowledge without distinction is not knowledge at all.

In this brief objection-and-answer exchange, the author defends a fundamental premise: that any object of knowledge must be distinct from other objects. He argues that this is not merely an adopted principle but a self-evident truth that no rational person can deny.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal English translation. Let the structure of the objection and answer become clear.
2
Understand the argument
The author states the premise is self-evident. Notice the reasoning: if knowledge does not distinguish the known from others, one is oblivious (غافل). Grasp why this makes the premise undeniable.
The concept of obliviousness is key—it shows that without distinction, no genuine knowledge occurs.
3
Reflect on self-evidence
Ask yourself: Can you think of any example of knowledge where the known is not distinguished from everything else? Why is this distinction necessary? This helps internalize why the author calls it self-evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولم قلتم (۳) : إن المعلوم يجب كونه متميزاً عن غيره : ؟
قلنا: هذه المقدمة بديهية. لأن العلم بالشيء [إن(۸)] لم يميز بين ذلك المعلوم وبين غيره ، كان غافلاً عنه ، من حيث إنه هو . ومن كان كذلك
His saying: 'Why did you say that the known must be distinguished from other things?' We said: This premise is self-evident. Because if knowledge of a thing does not distinguish between that known and other things, one would be oblivious to it as it is. And one who is thus...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a short objection-and-answer exchange. The objector asks why the known must be distinct from other objects of knowledge. The author replies that this premise is self-evident (بديهية). The reasoning: if knowledge of a thing does not set it apart from other things, then one is not actually knowing it but rather is oblivious to it. The essential condition of knowledge is that the known is recognized as this specific thing, not something else. The text ends mid-sentence, but the argument is complete in meaning.

Key Terms
تميز
بديهية
غفلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the Quran commands it
D) Because it follows from the study of physics

Key Takeaway
The distinction of the known from other things is a self-evident premise because without it, knowledge would collapse into obliviousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why Distinction in Pure Negation Is Impossible
The author refutes an objection about distinction in pure negation with two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two arguments that pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) cannot have distinction, and why distinction inherently requires positive reality.

Core Idea
Pure negation lacks any reality, so distinction is impossible.

This lesson addresses a critical objection in Islamic theology: if pure negation (like 'not existing') is a concept, can it be distinct from another negation? The author presents two decisive arguments that challenge the very possibility of distinguishing pure negations. Let's examine them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first argument
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the objection and the first argument (الأول). Understand that distinction requires each thing to be a reality in itself, which pure negation is not.
The first argument is a direct logical deduction.
2
Understand why distinction requires reality
Reflect on the point: for two things to be distinct, each must have a positive reality (ḥaqīqa) that differs from others. Pure negation has no essence, so it cannot serve as a basis for distinction.
3
Examine the second argument
Now read the second argument (الثاني). Even if we could conceive of distinction in pure negation, we know by intuition that actual things (world, man, horse) are positive realities, not pure negations.
This argument appeals to immediate, necessary knowledge.
4
Reflect on the counterexample
Consider the examples given: the world, the sphere, man, and horse. Their distinctness is self-evident. This confirms that they are not pure negations but determinate essences.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Combine both arguments: the first shows logical impossibility, the second shows empirical impossibility. The objection is thus refuted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في السؤال الرابع: «لم قلتم: إن حصول الامتياز في النفي المحض: محال؟» قلنا: هذا السؤال مدفوع من وجهين: الأول: إنه لا معنى لكون هذه الحقيقة، ممتازة عن تلك الحقيقة الأخرى، إلا أن هذه الحقيقة في نفسها، ليست مثل الحقيقة الأخرى. فما لم يكن ذلك الشيء في نفسه حقيقة من الحقائق، ومخالفة لسائر الحقائق، لم يكن الامتياز [حاصل]. وأما السلب المحض والنفي الصرف، فهو الذي لا يكون في نفسه حقيقة ولا ماهية أصلا. فيثبت: أن كونه نفياً محضاً، ينافي حصول الامتياز. الثاني: هب أنه يعقل الامتياز والاختلاف في النفي المحض والسلب الصرف، إلا [أنا] نعلم بالبديهة أن حقيقة العالم والفلك والإنسان، والفرس: ليست سلوباً محضة، وإعداماً صرفة. بل هذه الحقائق [حقائق] متعينة، وماهيات متمايزة. وكل واحد منها يخالف الحقيقة الأخرى بتعينها وتميزها، العلم بأن الأمر كذلك علم ضروري. غزال هذا السؤال.
As for his statement in the fourth question: 'Why did you say that the occurrence of distinction in pure negation is impossible?' We say: This question is refuted in two ways: First: There is no meaning to this reality being distinct from that other reality except that this reality in itself is not like the other reality. So as long as that thing is not in itself a reality among realities and different from all realities, distinction is not obtained. As for pure negation and absolute privation, it is that which is in itself neither a reality nor an essence at all. So it is established that its being pure negation is incompatible with the occurrence of distinction. Second: Even if distinction and difference in pure negation and absolute privation were conceivable, we know by intuition that the realities of the world, the sphere, man, and the horse are not pure privations and absolute non-existences. Rather, these realities are determinate realities and distinct essences. Each one of them differs from the other reality by its determinateness and distinction. Knowledge that this is the case is necessary knowledge. This question is removed.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector asks why distinction in pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) is impossible. The author provides two answers. First, distinction requires that each thing be a reality (ḥaqīqa) in itself, differing from others. Pure negation lacks any essence or reality, so it cannot be distinct. Second, even if we granted conceivability, we know by intuition (bi-l-badīha) that actual existents (world, sphere, man, horse) are not negations but determinate realities with distinct essences. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
نفي محض
سلب صرف
حقيقة
ماهية
امتياز
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pure negations can be distinct even without essence, e.g., 'not-A' and 'not-B' are different.
Correction: The author argues that distinction inherently requires a positive reality that differs. 'Not-A' and 'not-B' are not distinct in themselves; their apparent difference comes from the positive realities they negate.

Checkpoint

A) Pure negation is impossible to conceive.

C) Pure negation is the same as existence.
D) The author does not provide a first argument.

Key Takeaway
Pure negation cannot be distinct because distinction requires positive reality, and intuition confirms that existent things are not pure negations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Author announces refutation of fifth question.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Non-existent things cannot be considered realities; the author refutes this claim in two ways.

We encounter the fifth question: can non-existent essences still be called things and realities? The objector argues yes, but the author disagrees. In this passage, the author states that the question is refuted in two ways, though the full refutation is cut short. Let's explore what is presented.

This is a key position in the debate about the status of non-existent entities.

Two Types of Knowledge: Necessary and Theoretical
Author classifies knowledge into necessary and theoretical, proves existence of self-evident truths via regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the division of knowledge into necessary (intuitive) and theoretical, and why there must be self-evident truths as a foundation for all knowledge.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge needs proof; some truths are self-evident and foundational.

This lesson presents a key argument in Islamic epistemology: the classification of knowledge into two types. The author shows that if all knowledge required proof, it would lead to an impossible infinite regress. Therefore, there must be basic truths that are known directly without any need for demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the author's precise wording for 'necessary' and 'theoretical' knowledge.
Pay attention to the terms 'ḍarūriyya' and 'naẓariyya'.
2
Follow the regress argument
The author argues that if all knowledge were theoretical, each piece would require prior knowledge, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. Both are impossible, so some knowledge must be self-evident.
Think of this as a 'foundation' argument: you cannot have an infinite chain of justification.
3
Identify the criterion for self-evident truths
The author states that self-evident truths are those universally acknowledged by sound intellects without any proof. This provides a practical test for what counts as 'necessary' knowledge.
Examples include the law of non-contradiction or that the whole is greater than its part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة: أن قالوا: إنا نبين أن العلوم على قسمين: ضرورية بديهية لا يرتاب العقل فيها، ولا يفتقر في إثباتها إلى دليل وحجة. ونظرية، هي التي تكون محتاجة إلى الدليل والنظر. والذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك: أنا لا نشك في أنا نعلم أمراً من الأمور، وشيئاً من الأشياء، فنقول: ذلك العلم إما أن يكون نظرياً أو بديهياً، فإن كان الأول، افتقر ذلك العلم النظري إلى تقدم علوم أخرى عليه. والكلام فيها كما في الأول. ويلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل، وهما محالان. فوجب الاعتراف بوجود علوم بديهية ضرورية، يجب الاعتراف بها لا لأجل حجة وبينة ودليل، بل يجب الاعتراف بها لأعيانها ولذواتها. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إذا أردنا أن نعرف أن تلك العلوم البديهية، ما هي؟ وكيف هي؟ قلنا: إنا إذا عرضنا قضية من القضايا على العقول السليمة، والطباع المستقيمة، فإذا رأينا أصحابها مطبقين على الاعتراف بها من غير شك ولا شبهة، ومن غير حاجة فيها إلى ذكر دليل وحجة، فذلك هو العلم البديهي الضروري الأزلي. ويكون جزم العاقل بأمثال هذه القضايا، غنياً عن ذكر الحجة والبيئة والدليل.
The third proof: They said: We show that knowledge (al-ʿulūm) is of two types: necessary/intuitive (ḍarūriyya badīhiyya) which the intellect does not doubt and which does not need a proof or argument for its establishment, and theoretical (naẓariyya) which depends on proof and reflection. What indicates that the matter is so is that we do not doubt that we know something, so we say: That knowledge is either theoretical or intuitive. If it is the first, that theoretical knowledge depends on other knowledge preceding it. The discussion about that is as in the first. This leads to either circularity (al-dawr) or infinite regress (al-tasalsul), both of which are impossible. So it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of intuitive necessary knowledge that must be acknowledged not because of a proof, evidence, or argument, but must be acknowledged for its own essence. When this is established, we say: If we want to know what those intuitive sciences are and how they are, we say: When we present a proposition to sound intellects and upright natures, and we see their possessors unanimously acknowledge it without doubt or suspicion, and without needing to mention a proof or argument, then that is the intuitive, necessary, eternal knowledge. And the certainty of the rational person about such propositions is independent of mentioning proof, evidence, and argument.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage classifies knowledge into two types: necessary/intuitive and theoretical. The proof is by regress: if all knowledge were theoretical, it would require prior knowledge, leading to infinite regress or circularity, which are impossible. Therefore, there must be some foundational self-evident truths. The criterion for identifying such truths is that they are unanimously acknowledged by sound intellects without need for proof. These self-evident truths are the foundation for all knowledge. This is a standard argument in Islamic epistemology (usul al-fiqh and kalam). The author uses this to set the stage for the next argument.

Key Terms
علم ضروري
علم نظري
بديهي
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowledge must be proven by argument.
Correction: Some knowledge is self-evident and does not require proof; otherwise, an infinite regress of proofs would occur, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if all knowledge were theoretical?
A) All knowledge would be certain
B) There would be no need for proof
C) Circularity or infinite regress would result
D) Knowledge would be impossible

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is either self-evident (necessary) or dependent on proof (theoretical); without self-evident truths, all knowledge would be impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in God, but have you ever examined whether that belief rests on a foundation of self-evident truths or merely on habit? Today, identify one belief you hold that you have never questioned. Is it necessary knowledge or mere assumption? Resolve to test it against the criterion of sound intellects from this lesson.
?
The author argues that without self-evident truths, all knowledge would collapse into infinite regress. Consider your own path to certainty: are there truths you accept without evidence that you are unwilling to let go? What would it cost you if those truths turned out to be false? Will you honestly confront that fear today?
?
You have read that necessary knowledge is acknowledged by sound intellects without doubt. Is there any area of your life where you resist a self-evident truth because you are attached to a comfortable falsehood? What is it? And what would it take for you to surrender that resistance?

Proof of Necessary Dimensions
Sanusi proves that dimensions are necessary existents via a thought experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author proves that dimensions are necessary existents using the thought experiment of an animal at the edge of the world.

Core Idea
The distinction of sides at the world's edge proves dimensions are necessary.

Sanusi demonstrates that dimensions cannot be merely contingent by considering the necessary distinction of directions at the boundary of the cosmos. This argument relies on the immediate judgment of the innate intellect, showing that dimensions must be real and eternal. Let us follow the reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the thought experiment
Read the passage where the author asks us to imagine an animal standing at the edge of the world. Notice the two alternatives presented.
2
Identify the necessary judgment
The author argues that it is impossible for sides not to be distinguished. The innate nature of the soul witnesses this distinction.
This is a key premise.
3
Understand the conclusion
The distinction implies the existence of dimensions (length, width, depth). Since they cannot be annihilated, they are necessary existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الفرض وجب أن لا يلزم المحال . إلا أن المحال لازم لا محالة ، لأن عند ذلك الفرض ، إذا فرضنا حيواناً واقفاً على طرف العالم فإما أن يتميز الجانب الذي يلي وجهه عن الجانب الذي يلي قفاه ، وإما أن لا يتميز . والقسم الثاني مدفوع في بديهة العقل . لأن فطرة النفس شاهدة بأن على جميع التقديرات ، فإنه لابد وأن يتميز الجانب الأيمن عن الجانب الأيسر ، والجانب الذي يحاذي الوجه ، عن الجانب الذي يحاذي القفا . والقول بأنه تحصل حالة لا يحصل معها هذا الامتياز ، مما لا يقبله العقل ، وإذا ثبت حصول هذا الامتياز ، فقد حصلت الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً وعمقاً . فيثبت : أن هذه الأبعاد موجودة ، وأنها غير قابلة للعدم البتة ، فكانت واجبة الوجود لذواتها . فإن قيل : الكلام على هذا التقدير من وجهين : الوجه الأول : وهو قول الحكماء : وهو أن الأبعاد متناهية ، وخارج العالم لا خلاء ولا ملاء . وقولكم : بأن الواقف على طرف العالم ، لابد وأن يتميز فوقه عن تحته ، ويمينه عن شماله. فنقول : هذا الحكم وإن كان ضروري الثبوت في فطرة النفس ، إلا أنه حكم الوهم والخيال وقد يكون كاذباً غير ملتفت إليه فيثبت : أن هذا الحكم ، وإن كان واجب الثبوت في فطرة النفس ، إلا أنه غير مقبول . والوجه الثاني : وهو قول المتكلمين : وهو أن الامتياز في هذه الأحياز ، وفي هذه الجهات أمر حاصل لا يمكن إنكاره . إلا أن هذه الأحياز أشياء يفرضها العقل ، ويقدرها الوهم، وليس لها في نفسها وجود ولا ثبوت . والدليل عليه : وهو أنه لا معنى لهذا الشيء ، إلا أنه خلاء خالي ، وفضاء لم يحصل فيه شيء من الأجسام، فهذا عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . وليس له من الوجود البتة حظ . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : إنكم سلمتم أن الفطرة الأصلية شاهدة بصحة هذه القضية . فنقول : قولهم : إن في الفطرة حاكمان : أحدهما : العقل وحكمه صحيح . والثاني : الوهم ، وحكمه باطل . فنقول : علمنا بأن أحد الحاكمين صادق والآخر كاذب . إن كان علماً بديهياً فيلزم أن لا يحصل الجزم القاطع في حكم الحاكم الكاذب . وإن كان فطرياً فحينئذ تتوقف صحة البديهيات على هذه المقدمة النظرية . ولا شك أنها موقوفة على القضايا البديهية. فيلزم وقوع الدور ، وإنه باطل . فيثبت : أن قول القائل : إن الأحكام الفطرية ، إن كانت من نتائج العقل فهي صحيحة. وإن كانت من نتائج الوهم ، فهي باطلة توجب السفسطة، وتوجب القدح في جميع العلوم البديهية وإنه باطل. فيثبت : أن كل ما اعترف به أول الفطرة السليمة ، وجب الحكم بصحته قطعاً . وتمام الكلام في هذا الباب : مذكور في المنطق ، عند الفرق بين المقدمات البديهية ، وبين المقدمات الأولية . والجواب عن السؤال الثاني : إن الجسم إذا كان حاصلا في الحيز ، قبل فرض الفارضين ، واعتبار المعتبرين ، وبعد ذلك . وجب الحكم بأن الحيز أمر
This supposition must not entail the impossible. But the impossible is necessarily entailed, because upon that supposition, if we suppose an animal standing at the edge of the world, then either the side facing its face is distinguished from the side facing its back, or it is not distinguished. The second alternative is rejected by the primary intellect, because the innate nature of the soul witnesses that according to all estimations, the right side must be distinguished from the left side, and the side facing the face from the side facing the back. The statement that a state occurs in which this distinction does not occur is something the intellect does not accept. And when the occurrence of this distinction is established, then extending dimensions in length, width, and depth have occurred. So it is established that these dimensions exist, that they are absolutely incapable of non-existence, and thus they are necessary of existence in themselves. Then it is said: The discussion on this estimation has two aspects: The first aspect: the saying of the philosophers, that dimensions are finite, and beyond the world there is neither void nor plenum. And your statement that one standing at the edge of the world must distinguish above from below and right from left. We say: This judgment, though it is necessarily established in the innate nature of the soul, yet it is a judgment of estimation and imagination, and may be false and disregarded. So it is established that this judgment, though necessarily established in the innate nature, is nonetheless unacceptable. The second aspect: the saying of the mutakallimun, that distinction in these spaces and in these directions is something obtained that cannot be denied, but these spaces are things that the intellect supposes and the estimation postulates, having no existence or reality in themselves. The proof of that: that this thing means nothing but an empty void, a space in which nothing of bodies has occurred; thus it is pure non-existence and bare negation. It has no share of existence at all. The answer to the first question: We say: You have conceded that the original innate nature witnesses the truth of this proposition. We say: Their statement is that in the innate nature there are two judges: one is the intellect and its judgment is true; the second is the estimation and its judgment is false. We say: Our knowledge that one of the two judges is truthful and the other is false; if it is intuitive knowledge, then decisive certainty in the judgment of the false judge would not be obtained; and if it is innate (but theoretical), then the soundness of intuitions would depend on this theoretical premise, and there is no doubt that they depend on intuitive propositions, so circularity would occur, and that is invalid. So it is established that the statement of the one who says: 'Innate judgments, if they are from the results of the intellect, are true; and if they are from the results of the estimation, are false' leads to sophistry and leads to impugning all intuitive and theoretical sciences, and that is invalid. So it is established that everything that is acknowledged by the sound innate nature at the first (intuition) must be judged as certainly true. The complete discussion on this topic is mentioned in logic, in the distinction between intuitive premises and primary premises. The answer to the second question: That if a body exists in space, before the supposition of supposers and the consideration of considerers, and after that, it must be judged that space is a thing...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation unit. The author first establishes that if we suppose an animal at the edge of the world, the sides (front/back, right/left) must be distinguished. This distinction implies the existence of dimensions (length, width, depth). Since these dimensions cannot be destroyed, they are necessary existents in themselves. Then he presents two objections. The first, from the philosophers, argues that the judgment of distinction, though seemingly necessary, comes from the imagination (wahm) and may be false, so it is unacceptable as a proof. The second, from the mutakallimun, concedes the distinction but says the spaces (ahyaz) in which the distinction occurs are mere mental constructs with no real existence; they are pure non-existence (void). In answer to the first objection, the author argues that if we say there are two judges in innate nature—intellect (true) and imagination (false)—then our knowledge that one is true and the other false cannot be intuitive without undermining certainty about the false one, nor can it be theoretical without causing circularity (since theoretical premises depend on intuitions). Hence, the only consistent position is to accept the original intuitive judgment as certainly true. The answer to the second objection begins but is cut off: it argues that if a body exists in a space, before and after any consideration, the space must be a real thing (not mere nothing). The text ends before completing this argument.

Key Terms
أبعاد
فطرة
عقل
وجوب الذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the distinction at the edge of the world is merely a product of imagination and not a real, necessary truth.
Correction: The author argues that the judgment is a primary intellectual intuition, not a mere estimation, and thus it is necessarily true. Dismissing it leads to skepticism.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main conclusion from the thought experiment?
A) The world is infinite
B) Dimensions exist and are necessary
C) The animal cannot stand at the edge
D) Sides are not really distinguished

Key Takeaway
The necessary distinction of sides at the world's edge proves that dimensions are real and necessary existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you think about your physical existence and the space you occupy, do you truly acknowledge that your perception of dimensions is not just a mental construct but reflects a necessary reality? How does this affect your attitude toward the physical world and its ultimate dependence on the Creator?
?
The proof uses the innate nature of the soul (fiṭrah) as witness. When was the last time you allowed your innermost nature—untrained by culture or habit—to judge a truth about existence? What would change in your life if you began to trust that deep-seated recognition of necessity in matters of faith?
?
Sanusi says dimensions are 'absolutely incapable of non-existence.' This means the space you occupy now is not merely accidental but carries a kind of necessity. Does this make you feel more secure in your surroundings, or does it unsettle you because it implies a fixed order beyond your control? How will you respond to the idea that even the space you live in is a sign of a necessary, enduring reality?

Refutation of Two Objections on Dimensions
Sanusi answers two objections regarding the proof of necessary dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two objections to the necessity of dimensions and the author's answers, especially the critique of the imagination/intellect distinction.

Core Idea
Dismissing intuitive judgments as imagination leads to skepticism and circularity.

After proving dimensions are necessary, Sanusi anticipates two objections: one from philosophers dismissing the judgment as imagination, and one from mutakallimun denying the reality of spaces. His answer to the first is a powerful argument against skepticism. Let us explore these objections and responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the passage that presents the two objections: (1) from philosophers saying the judgment is from imagination and possibly false, (2) from mutakallimun saying spaces are mental constructs.
2
Analyze the answer to the first objection
The author's response: if we say there are two judges in innate nature (intellect true, imagination false), then our knowledge that one is true and the other false must be intuitive or theoretical. If intuitive, we cannot be certain about the false judgment; if theoretical, it causes circularity. Therefore, we must accept the original judgment as true.
This is a key logical argument.
3
Note the incomplete answer to the second objection
The author begins an answer: if a body exists in space, then space must be something real. The rest is cut off, so we only have the beginning.
The passage ends incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا الفرض وجب أن لا يلزم المحال . إلا أن المحال لازم لا محالة ، لأن عند ذلك الفرض ، إذا فرضنا حيواناً واقفاً على طرف العالم فإما أن يتميز الجانب الذي يلي وجهه عن الجانب الذي يلي قفاه ، وإما أن لا يتميز . والقسم الثاني مدفوع في بديهة العقل . لأن فطرة النفس شاهدة بأن على جميع التقديرات ، فإنه لابد وأن يتميز الجانب الأيمن عن الجانب الأيسر ، والجانب الذي يحاذي الوجه ، عن الجانب الذي يحاذي القفا . والقول بأنه تحصل حالة لا يحصل معها هذا الامتياز ، مما لا يقبله العقل ، وإذا ثبت حصول هذا الامتياز ، فقد حصلت الأبعاد الممتدة طولاً وعرضاً وعمقاً . فيثبت : أن هذه الأبعاد موجودة ، وأنها غير قابلة للعدم البتة ، فكانت واجبة الوجود لذواتها . فإن قيل : الكلام على هذا التقدير من وجهين : الوجه الأول : وهو قول الحكماء : وهو أن الأبعاد متناهية ، وخارج العالم لا خلاء ولا ملاء . وقولكم : بأن الواقف على طرف العالم ، لابد وأن يتميز فوقه عن تحته ، ويمينه عن شماله. فنقول : هذا الحكم وإن كان ضروري الثبوت في فطرة النفس ، إلا أنه حكم الوهم والخيال وقد يكون كاذباً غير ملتفت إليه فيثبت : أن هذا الحكم ، وإن كان واجب الثبوت في فطرة النفس ، إلا أنه غير مقبول . والوجه الثاني : وهو قول المتكلمين : وهو أن الامتياز في هذه الأحياز ، وفي هذه الجهات أمر حاصل لا يمكن إنكاره . إلا أن هذه الأحياز أشياء يفرضها العقل ، ويقدرها الوهم، وليس لها في نفسها وجود ولا ثبوت . والدليل عليه : وهو أنه لا معنى لهذا الشيء ، إلا أنه خلاء خالي ، وفضاء لم يحصل فيه شيء من الأجسام، فهذا عدم محض ، ونفي صرف . وليس له من الوجود البتة حظ . والجواب عن السؤال الأول : أن نقول : إنكم سلمتم أن الفطرة الأصلية شاهدة بصحة هذه القضية . فنقول : قولهم : إن في الفطرة حاكمان : أحدهما : العقل وحكمه صحيح . والثاني : الوهم ، وحكمه باطل . فنقول : علمنا بأن أحد الحاكمين صادق والآخر كاذب . إن كان علماً بديهياً فيلزم أن لا يحصل الجزم القاطع في حكم الحاكم الكاذب . وإن كان فطرياً فحينئذ تتوقف صحة البديهيات على هذه المقدمة النظرية . ولا شك أنها موقوفة على القضايا البديهية. فيلزم وقوع الدور ، وإنه باطل . فيثبت : أن قول القائل : إن الأحكام الفطرية ، إن كانت من نتائج العقل فهي صحيحة. وإن كانت من نتائج الوهم ، فهي باطلة توجب السفسطة، وتوجب القدح في جميع العلوم البديهية وإنه باطل. فيثبت : أن كل ما اعترف به أول الفطرة السليمة ، وجب الحكم بصحته قطعاً . وتمام الكلام في هذا الباب : مذكور في المنطق ، عند الفرق بين المقدمات البديهية ، وبين المقدمات الأولية . والجواب عن السؤال الثاني : إن الجسم إذا كان حاصلا في الحيز ، قبل فرض الفارضين ، واعتبار المعتبرين ، وبعد ذلك . وجب الحكم بأن الحيز أمر
This supposition must not entail the impossible. But the impossible is necessarily entailed, because upon that supposition, if we suppose an animal standing at the edge of the world, then either the side facing its face is distinguished from the side facing its back, or it is not distinguished. The second alternative is rejected by the primary intellect, because the innate nature of the soul witnesses that according to all estimations, the right side must be distinguished from the left side, and the side facing the face from the side facing the back. The statement that a state occurs in which this distinction does not occur is something the intellect does not accept. And when the occurrence of this distinction is established, then extending dimensions in length, width, and depth have occurred. So it is established that these dimensions exist, that they are absolutely incapable of non-existence, and thus they are necessary of existence in themselves. Then it is said: The discussion on this estimation has two aspects: The first aspect: the saying of the philosophers, that dimensions are finite, and beyond the world there is neither void nor plenum. And your statement that one standing at the edge of the world must distinguish above from below and right from left. We say: This judgment, though it is necessarily established in the innate nature of the soul, yet it is a judgment of estimation and imagination, and may be false and disregarded. So it is established that this judgment, though necessarily established in the innate nature, is nonetheless unacceptable. The second aspect: the saying of the mutakallimun, that distinction in these spaces and in these directions is something obtained that cannot be denied, but these spaces are things that the intellect supposes and the estimation postulates, having no existence or reality in themselves. The proof of that: that this thing means nothing but an empty void, a space in which nothing of bodies has occurred; thus it is pure non-existence and bare negation. It has no share of existence at all. The answer to the first question: We say: You have conceded that the original innate nature witnesses the truth of this proposition. We say: Their statement is that in the innate nature there are two judges: one is the intellect and its judgment is true; the second is the estimation and its judgment is false. We say: Our knowledge that one of the two judges is truthful and the other is false; if it is intuitive knowledge, then decisive certainty in the judgment of the false judge would not be obtained; and if it is innate (but theoretical), then the soundness of intuitions would depend on this theoretical premise, and there is no doubt that they depend on intuitive propositions, so circularity would occur, and that is invalid. So it is established that the statement of the one who says: 'Innate judgments, if they are from the results of the intellect, are true; and if they are from the results of the estimation, are false' leads to sophistry and leads to impugning all intuitive and theoretical sciences, and that is invalid. So it is established that everything that is acknowledged by the sound innate nature at the first (intuition) must be judged as certainly true. The complete discussion on this topic is mentioned in logic, in the distinction between intuitive premises and primary premises. The answer to the second question: That if a body exists in space, before the supposition of supposers and the consideration of considerers, and after that, it must be judged that space is a thing...
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation unit. The author first establishes that if we suppose an animal at the edge of the world, the sides (front/back, right/left) must be distinguished. This distinction implies the existence of dimensions (length, width, depth). Since these dimensions cannot be destroyed, they are necessary existents in themselves. Then he presents two objections. The first, from the philosophers, argues that the judgment of distinction, though seemingly necessary, comes from the imagination (wahm) and may be false, so it is unacceptable as a proof. The second, from the mutakallimun, concedes the distinction but says the spaces (ahyaz) in which the distinction occurs are mere mental constructs with no real existence; they are pure non-existence (void). In answer to the first objection, the author argues that if we say there are two judges in innate nature—intellect (true) and imagination (false)—then our knowledge that one is true and the other false cannot be intuitive without undermining certainty about the false one, nor can it be theoretical without causing circularity (since theoretical premises depend on intuitions). Hence, the only consistent position is to accept the original intuitive judgment as certainly true. The answer to the second objection begins but is cut off: it argues that if a body exists in a space, before and after any consideration, the space must be a real thing (not mere nothing). The text ends before completing this argument.

Key Terms
وهم
حيز
خلاء
فطرة
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the first objection can be accepted without consequences because the judgment is merely from imagination.
Correction: The author shows that dismissing an intuitive judgment as imagination leads to either uncertainty about all such judgments or circularity, undermining all knowledge.

Checkpoint
What problem does the author identify with the claim that there are two judges (intellect true, imagination false) in innate nature?
A) It contradicts the philosophers' view
B) It leads to either lack of certainty about the false judgment or circularity
C) It proves that the judgment of distinction is false
D) It shows that the mutakallimun are correct

Key Takeaway
Dismissing an intuitive judgment as imagination leads to skepticism or circularity, so the sound innate nature's judgment must be accepted as certainly true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear truth of your conscience as 'just my imagination' because someone told you it wasn't rational? How does this passage empower you to trust the sound innate nature against such skepticism?
?
In which areas of your life have you allowed a false dichotomy between 'intellect' and 'emotion' to undermine your certainty in foundational moral truths? What would it cost you to fully accept the judgment of your sound fiṭra?
?
This refutation warns that rejecting fiṭra leads to either loss of certainty or circular reasoning. Is there a belief you hold that you are afraid to examine because it might fall apart? What would it mean for your relationship with Allah if you submitted to the clear judgment of your innate nature?

The Proof Against the Body's Eternity: Motion and Rest in Pre-Eternity
The author outlines a proof against the body's eternity and details its three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the kalam proof against the eternity of the body, including its three premises and the justification of the first premise.

Core Idea
A body cannot be eternal because it must be moving or static, and neither is possible in pre-eternity.

This passage presents one of the classic proofs in Islamic theology for the temporal origination of the world (huduth). The author begins by stating a conditional argument: if a body were eternal, it would have to be either moving or stationary in pre-eternity. Both options are shown to be impossible, so the body cannot be eternal. The author then identifies three premises that must be established to complete the proof and begins explaining the first premise. As you read, pay attention to the logical progression and the key concepts of space (hayz) and occupying space (mutahayyiz).

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to get the overall flow. Notice that the author first states a conditional argument, then breaks it down into three premises.
The argument is in the form of a conditional syllogism.
2
Identify the three premises
Locate where the author enumerates the three premises. Understand that the first premise establishes the dichotomy of motion and rest. The second and third premises will show the impossibility of each in pre-eternity.
3
Examine the first premise's justification
Look at how the author justifies the first premise: every body occupies space, so it is either fixed in that space (stationary) or not fixed (moving). This establishes the necessary dichotomy.
The author uses the concept of 'mutahayyiz' (occupying space).
4
Reflect on the argument's significance
Consider why this argument is important for proving that the world is created (huduth). The impossibility of eternal motion or rest leads to the body having a beginning in time.
This is a standard proof in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في تقرير حفه الحجة
نقول : لو كان الجسم أزلياً ، لكان في الأزل . إما أن يكون متحركاً ، أو ساكناً . والقسمان باطلان ، فيمتنع كونه أزلياً
واعلم : أنا نفتقر في تقرير هذا الدليل، إلى إثبات مقدمات ثلاثة : أولها : إن الجسم متى كان موجوداً ، وجب أن يكون إما متحركاً ، وإما (١) ساكناً .
وثانيها : [ بيان (۲) ] أن الجسم يمتنع أن يكون [ في الأزل (۳) ] متحركاً .
وثالثها : بيان أنه يمتنع كونه ساكناً .
وحينئذ يتم الدليل . أما بيان المقدمة الأولى (٤) : فهو أنا نقول : لا شك أن كل ما كان متحيزاً ، وجب أن يكون حاصلاً في حيز معين . وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : إما أن يكون مستقراً في ذلك الحيز ، وإما أن لا يكون كذلك ، بل يكون متنقلاً من حيز إلى حيز آخر . والأول هو الساكن ، والثاني هو
The first chapter on establishing the aspect of the proof.
We say: If the body were eternal, it would be in pre-eternity. Either it is moving or it is stationary. Both divisions are false, so its being eternal is impossible.
Know that in establishing this proof we need to prove three premises: The first: Whenever a body exists, it must be either moving or stationary. The second: [An exposition] that a body cannot be moving [in pre-eternity]. The third: An exposition that it cannot be stationary. Then the proof is complete. As for the exposition of the first premise: it is that we say: There is no doubt that everything that occupies space (mutahayyiz) must be located in a specific space (hayz). If this is established, we say: Either it is stable in that space, or it is not so, but rather moves from one space to another. The first is the stationary, and the second is the moving.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalil) against the eternity of the body (jism), which is a standard argument in kalam theology for establishing the temporal origination of the world (huduth). The author begins by stating the core argument: if a body were eternal (azali), it would have existed in pre-eternity (al-azal). In that state, it must be either moving (mutaharrik) or stationary (sakin). Both possibilities are shown to be impossible, therefore the body cannot be eternal. The author then notes that this argument relies on proving three premises. The first premise is that any existing body must be either moving or stationary. The second premise is that a body cannot be moving in pre-eternity. The third is that it cannot be stationary in pre-eternity. After establishing these, the proof is complete. The passage then elaborates on the first premise: everything that is extended in space (mutahayyiz) must occupy a specific place (hayz). Given that, it is either fixed in that place (i.e., stationary) or it is not fixed, meaning it moves from one place to another (i.e., moving). This establishes the dichotomy necessary for the first premise. The author follows the logical structure of conditional reasoning, typical of the kalam method.

Key Terms
أزلي
متحرك
ساكن
حيز
متحيز
دليل
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument assumes motion and rest are the only possible states, implying that eternal motion or rest is inherently contradictory.
Correction: The author does not claim that motion or rest are inherently contradictory, but rather that specific natures of eternal motion or rest are impossible, which will be shown in the later premises.

Checkpoint
What are the three premises needed to prove that a body cannot be eternal?
A) The body is composite; it is either created or uncreated; it is either finite or infinite.
B) The body must be either moving or stationary; it cannot be moving in pre-eternity; it cannot be stationary in pre-eternity.
C) The body is contingent; it requires a creator; the creator must be eternal.
D) The body occupies space; space is finite; time is finite.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that a body cannot be eternal because it would require an impossible state of eternal motion or rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that your own body, like all bodies, had a beginning. If you truly believed that your existence is not eternal, how would that change the way you use your time today? Will you live today as if it is a finite gift, or as if you have forever?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of eternal motion or rest. Are there areas in your life—sinful habits, spiritual stagnation—where you have assumed you can remain 'stationary' forever? What would it mean to break that false stillness and move towards repentance, knowing you are not eternal?
?
If the world has a beginning, it must have a Beginner. Does your heart acknowledge that your existence is a deliberate act of creation? How would your daily actions change if you lived with the constant awareness that you are entirely dependent on the One who brought you from nothing?

Impossibility of Body in Non-Sensory Place
Author argues that body cannot be contained in a non-sensory hayyiz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational impossibility of a body being contained in a place that is either an accident or a non-sensory existent.

Core Idea
Body and place cannot mutually contain each other; containment requires sensory indication.

Sanusi continues his analysis of place (hayyiz). Having defined place as the containing entity, he now examines two problematic scenarios: place as an accident and place as a non-sensible existent. Both lead to contradictions, confirming that only a sensory substrate can contain a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice that Sanusi begins with 'And if the second' referring to a previous classification of place.
The passage is a continuation; the 'first' type of place was likely sensory place.
2
Identify the two cases
Find the two scenarios Sanusi considers: (1) place that is an accident (pointed to by accident), and (2) place that exists but is not sensorily indicated.
3
Understand the impossibility in each case
For case 1, ask why mutual containment is impossible. For case 2, note that sensory and non-sensory are separate, so containment fails.
Sanusi uses logical contradiction to show impossibility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with 'This is the discussion concerning place.' Consider that Sanusi has ruled out non-sensory containers for a body.
This reinforces the kalam definition of place as a sensory entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الثاني : وهو أن يكون الحيز مشار إليه بحسب التبعية ، فهذا هو العرض والعرض حاصل في الجسم، فلو كان الجسم حاصلا في العرض ، لزم كون كل واحد منها ظرفاً للآخر ومظروفاً له ، وهو محال . وأما إن كان المسمى بالحيز موجوداً ، غير مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، امتنع حصول الجسم فيه ، بمعنى كونه مظروفاً لذلك الشيء ، وكون ذلك الشيء ظرفاً للجسم ، لأن الجسم موجود مشار إليه بحسب الحس ، وإذا كان المسمى بالحيز موجوداً ، غير مشار إليه ، كان كل واحد منهما مبايناً عن الآخر وحينئذ يمتنع كونه ظرفاً له . فهذا هو الكلام في الحيز .
And if the second: that the place is pointed to by way of accident, then this is the accident, and the accident inheres in the body. So if the body were to inhere in the accident, then each of them would have to be a container for the other and contained in it, which is impossible. But if that which is called place exists, not being sensorily indicated, then it is impossible for the body to be contained in it, meaning that the body is contained by that thing and that thing is a container for the body, because the body exists as something sensorily indicated, and if the thing called place exists without being sensorily indicated, then each of them is separate from the other, and then it is impossible for it to be a container for it. This is the discussion concerning place.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument about the nature of place (hayyiz) and its relation to body. The author first considers the second type of place: that which is pointed to by accident (bi-ḥasab al-tabiʿiyya). This is identified as an accident (ʿaraḍ). Since an accident inheres in the body, if the body were to inhere in the accident, mutual containment would occur, which is impossible. Then the author considers another case: if the place is an existent that is not sensorily indicated, then since the body is sensorily indicated, they are separate and containment is impossible. The conclusion is that body cannot be contained in place in these senses.

Key Terms
حيز
عرض
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that body and place are independent entities that can contain each other.
Correction: The passage shows that if place is an accident, mutual containment is contradictory; if place is a non-sensory existent, separation prevents containment.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is impossible if the place is an accident?
A) Body can be contained in place
B) Place can be contained in body
C) Both containment scenarios are impossible
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
A body cannot be contained in a place that is either an accident or a non-sensory existent because such containment leads to logical contradiction or ontological separation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's existence, do you unconsciously assign a spatial location to Him, even though He transcends place? How does this logical proof help you correct that tendency today?
?
Consider a time you treated a non-physical concept (like 'luck' or 'fate') as if it had physical reality. How can the principle of ontological separation in this passage guard you against misapplying physical categories to spiritual truths?
?
If body cannot be contained in a non-sensory place, what does that imply about how you should approach the relationship between the material world and the unseen? What concrete step can you take to avoid confusing the two in your worship or daily life?
