Five Proofs for the Distinction of Spatial Occupation
Author enumerates five proofs for the distinction between a body's essence and its spatial location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five rational proofs that establish the distinction between a body's essence and its spatial location, and why this distinction is important in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A body's essence is distinct from its location; five proofs demonstrate this conceptual separation.

In this passage, the author presents the foundational argument that the spatial occupation (hayyiz) of a body is not identical to its essence. He offers five distinct proofs, each approaching the distinction from a different angle—conceptual, relational, linguistic, persistence through change, and incompatibility with motion. Understanding these proofs is essential for grasping later discussions on the nature of bodies and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the author enumerates five proofs (wujuh). Each proof is labeled 'first', 'second', etc. Try to grasp the main idea of each proof.
2
Identify the five proofs
List the five proofs: (1) conceptual separation, (2) essence vs. relation, (3) linguistic informativeness, (4) persistence of essence through change, (5) motion and rest compatibility. Compare them.
3
Reflect on the underlying principle
Ask yourself why proving distinction matters. How does it relate to the broader theological project of distinguishing between a thing and its attributes?
The author hints at deeper discussions in his other work.
4
Summarize in your own words
Write a short summary of each proof in your own words. This will help solidify your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الأول : إثبات المغايرة . والدليل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه يمكننا تعقل ذات ذلك الجسم ، مع الذهول عن كونه حاصلاً في ذلك الحيز . والمعلوم مغاير لما هو غير معلوم . والثاني : إن ذات الجسم قائمة بنفسها ، والمعقول من كونه حاصلا في ذلك الحيز المعين : نسبة مخصوصة ، وإضافة مخصوصة فتكون إحداهما مغايرة للأخرى . والثالث : إنا إذا قلنا : الجسم جسم. كان كلامنا مكرراً غير مفيد وإذا قلنا : الجسم حاصل في هذا الحيز . كان كلامنا مفيداً وذلك يوجب المغايرة . والرابع : إن الجسم إذا خرج عن ذلك الحيز الذي كان ساكناً فيه ، فإنه أن يقال : إن تلك الذات باقية ، إلا أن حصولها في ذلك الحيز غير باق والباقي مغاير لما هو غير باق . والخامس : إن الحركة منافية للسكون ، وغير منافية لذات الجسم وذلك يوجب التغاير، وفي هذه الدلائل مباحثات عميقة. ذكرناها في كتاب أحكام الوجود في باب : أن الوجود ، هل هو زائد على الماهية أم لا ؟
The first station: establishing the distinction. The proof for it has several aspects: First, we can conceive the essence of that body while being heedless of its being located in that place. And what is known is distinct from what is not known. Second, the essence of the body is self-subsistent, while what is understood from its being located in that specific place is a specific relation and a specific addition; thus one of them is distinct from the other. Third, when we say 'the body is a body,' our speech is repetitive and not informative; but when we say 'the body is located in this place,' our speech is informative, and that necessitates distinction. Fourth, if the body leaves that place in which it was stationary, it can be said that that essence persists, but its being located in that place does not persist, and the persisting is distinct from what is not persisting. Fifth, motion is incompatible with rest, but not incompatible with the essence of the body, and that necessitates distinction. And in these proofs there are profound investigations that we have mentioned in the book 'Ahkam al-Wujud' in the chapter on whether existence is additional to essence or not.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage presents the first station in proving that spatial occupation (al-hayyiz) is distinct from the essence of the body. The author offers five proofs. The first proof appeals to conceptual separation: we can think of the body's essence without considering its location, and what is known separately is distinct from what is not known. The second proof argues that the essence is self-subsistent while location is a relational attribute, so they are different. The third proof shows the difference through language: 'body' alone conveys no new information, but 'body in this place' does, indicating that location adds something distinct. The fourth proof uses change: when a body moves, its essence remains but its location changes, so the essence and location are distinct. The fifth proof contrasts motion and rest: motion contradicts rest but not the body's essence, so rest must be distinct from the essence. The author notes that deeper discussions of these proofs are in his other work.

Key Terms
المغايرة
ذات الجسم
الحيز
السكون
الحركة
النسبة المخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial occupation is identical to the body's essence.
Correction: These five proofs demonstrate that spatial occupation is conceptually distinct from the essence of the body, as shown through conceptual separation, relational nature, linguistic informativeness, persistence through change, and incompatibility with motion.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why does saying 'the body is in this place' imply distinction?
A) Because the word 'body' is repeated.
B) Because the statement becomes informative, unlike the tautology 'the body is a body'.
C) Because the body changes its location.
D) Because motion is incompatible with rest.

Key Takeaway
The five proofs collectively establish that a body's spatial location is not part of its essence but an additional attribute.

Rational Proof that Spatial Occupation is an Existing Attribute
Rational proof that spatial occupation is a real attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational contradiction between being in a place and not being in a place establishes that spatial occupation is a real, existing attribute.

Core Idea
Spatial occupation is real because its opposite is pure non-existence.

This lesson examines a concise rational proof from the kalam tradition. The author argues that the concept of being located cannot be a mere negation, because the intellect sees a clear contradiction between occupying space and not occupying it. Since non-occupation is pure non-existence, occupation must be something positive and existent. This passage establishes that rest (sukūn) is an actual attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the logical structure come through before interpreting.
Notice the compact argument style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify Key Terms
Identify terms like المفهوم المغاير (the distinct concept), أمر ثابت (fixed matter), صريح العقل (clear intellect), العدم المحض (pure non-existence), السكون (rest). Understand their meanings.
These terms anchor the proof.
3
Understand the Contradiction
The core premise: the intellect judges that being in a place and not being in a place are contradictory. Not being in a place is understood as pure non-existence, so its opposite must be something existent.
This is a rational, not empirical, proof.
4
Follow the Conclusion
The conclusion follows necessarily: the concept of being in a place is an existent attribute, identified as 'rest' (sukūn). The proof is complete.
The proof does not rely on scripture; it's purely rational.
5
Reflect on Significance
This proof establishes that spatial occupation is a real attribute, not a mere relation. This has implications for understanding the nature of bodies and motion in kalam.
Think about why the author needed to prove this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني : فهو بيان أن هذا المفهوم المغاير أمر ثابت . والدليل عليه أن صريح العقل، حكم بأن كونه حاصلاً في الحيز، مناقض لكونه : غير حاصل في الحيز ، والمعقول من كونه غير حاصل في الحيز : عدم محض ، فوجب أن يكون المفهوم من كونه حاصلاً في الحيز أمراً موجوداً . فثبت بهذه الوجوه : أن السكون صفة موجودة . وهذا تمام الكلام في إثبات هذا المطلوب [ والله أعلم ]
As for the second station, it is an exposition that this distinct concept is a fixed matter. The proof for it is that the clear intellect judges that its being located in a place contradicts its not being located in a place; and what is understood from its not being located in a place is pure non-existence. So it is necessary that the concept of its being located in a place is something existent. So it is established by these proofs that rest (sukun) is an existing attribute. And this is the complete speech in establishing this sought claim. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is the second station in proving that the distinct concept of spatial occupation is a real, existing attribute. The proof is based on the rational judgment that being located in a place is contradictory to not being located in that place. Since 'not being located' is pure non-existence (ʿadam maḥḍ), its opposite must be something existent. Therefore, the concept of being located (al-ḥāṣil fī al-ḥayyiz) is an existing attribute, which the author identifies as 'rest' (sukūn). The passage concludes the first issue with the affirmation that rest is an existent attribute.

Key Terms
المفهوم المغاير
أمر ثابت
صريح العقل
العدم المحض
السكون
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spatial location is merely a relative notion without real existence.
Correction: The proof shows that being in a place must be a real, positive attribute because its opposite (not being in a place) is pure non-existence, and contradictions require one side to be existent.

Checkpoint

C) By listing five earlier proofs.
D) By referring to his other book.

Key Takeaway
The distinct concept of spatial occupation (rest) is proven to be a real, existent attribute because it is the contradictory opposite of pure non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does the fact that you exist in a place—your body, your residence—serve as a constant reminder that Allah has granted you existence? Will you thank Him today for the gift of being, not non-being?
?
The intellect judges that being in a place contradicts not being in a place. Are there any contradictions in your life between what you claim to believe and how you live? Examine one area where your actions deny your faith, and commit today to resolving that contradiction.
?
The attribute of 'rest' is proven to be real because its opposite is nothing. Your own faith in Allah's oneness is a positive reality; its opposite, shirk, is pure falsehood. Do you treat your tawhid as a living, active reality in your daily decisions? What one step can you take today to make your faith more than just a concept in your mind?

The First Proof: Bodies Are Essentially Equal
First proof that bodies can change location, based on essential equality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the equality of all bodies in their complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya) serves as the basis for proving that any body can leave its specific location.

Core Idea
All bodies are essentially equal, so what is possible for one is possible for all.

This lesson examines the first proof offered by Imam al-Sanusi for the claim that every body can exit its specific location. The proof rests on a fundamental principle of kalam: the equality of similars. By analyzing the nature of bodies, the author shows that no intrinsic difference can prevent a body from moving, and any supposed difference falls into one of three untenable categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: the author states the claim, then presents the proof, then breaks down possible differences.
The passage is incomplete; the proof cuts off mid-argument.
2
Identify the three alternatives
The proof considers three ways bodies could differ beyond their essential equality. Find each one in the text.
The alternatives are: attributes of voluminousness, being an attribute in a substrate, or neither attribute nor substrate.
3
Understand why the first alternative fails
The first alternative is that differences come from attributes of voluminousness. Why is this invalid? Because similars must have the same possibilities.
If essences are equal, any attribute possible for one must be possible for all.
4
Understand why the second and third alternatives fail
The second reduces bodies to attributes in a substrate, which is absurd and will be refuted later. The third makes differences unrelated to essence, thus not causing differentiation.
The second alternative is invalid because it would make a body an attribute; the third is invalid because what is distinct from essence cannot cause difference.
5
Grasp the implicit conclusion
Although the passage cuts off, the intended conclusion is that no factor prevents a body from leaving its location, so it is possible.
The proof relies on the principle that if no difference exists, the possibility is universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : في بيان أن كل جسم ، فإنه يصح خروجه من حيزه المعين . ولنا في تقريره دلائل كثيرة : الحجة الأولى : إن كل جسم اختص بحيز معين ، فذلك الجسم : مساو لسائر الأجسام في تمام الماهية. ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، لزم إمكان خروج كل جسم عن حيزه المعين . إنما قلنا : الاجسام كلها متساوية في تمام الماهية . وذلك لأن الأجسام متساوية في كونها حجماً ومتحيز فلو فرضنا اختلافها في أمر آخر وراء هذا المعنى ، فذلك إما أن يكون من صفات الحجمية، أو من موصوفاتها ، أو لا صفة لها ولا موصوفاً بها . والأول باطل . لأن على هذا التقدير : ذوات الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية ، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، فكل صفة أمكن حصولها لبعض تلك الأشياء ، وجب إمكان حصولها لسائرها . ضرورة أن المتماثلات يجب استواؤها في كل اللوازم . والثاني : [باطل] وإلا لكان الجسم المتحيز : صفة [ حالة ] في محل ، وسنبين في مسألة : نفي الهيولى ) : أن هذا القول باطل محال . والثالث : أيضاً باطل لأن ما [ لا ] يكون صفة للجسم ، ولا موصوفاً به ، كان مبايناً عن ذات الجسم . وما كان كذلك ، لم يكن سبباً لاختلاف
The second issue: explaining that every body, its exiting from its specific location is possible. We have many proofs in establishing it: The first proof: that every body is distinguished by a specific location, that body is equal to all other bodies in the entirety of essence. And whenever the matter is thus, the possibility of every body exiting its specific location follows. We only said: all bodies are equal in the entirety of essence. And that is because bodies are equal in being volume and being extended [mutahayyiz]. So if we suppose they differ in something else beyond this meaning, then that is either from the attributes of voluminousness, or from their substrates, or neither an attribute for them nor a substrate for them. The first is invalid, because on this assumption the essences of bodies are equal in the entirety of essence, and whenever the matter is thus, every attribute that is possible to occur for some of those things, its possibility of occurring for the rest is necessary, because similars must be equal in all concomitants. The second is invalid [bāṭil], because then the extended body would be an attribute—a state—in a substrate, and we will explain in the issue of the negation of hyle that this statement is invalid and impossible. The third is also invalid, because whatever is not an attribute of the body nor a substrate for it is distinct from the essence of the body, and whatever is thus is not a cause for difference.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage introduces the second issue: proving that any body can leave its specific location. The author states there are many proofs, then presents the first proof (al-ḥujja al-ūlā). The proof hinges on the equality of all bodies in their complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya). Since bodies are equal in being volume and extended, any assumed difference must arise from one of three possibilities: (1) from attributes belonging to voluminousness, (2) from substrates that are attributes in a subject, or (3) from something neither an attribute nor a substrate. The first possibility is invalid because if essences are equal, then any attribute possible for one must be possible for all similars. The second is invalid because it would imply that a body is an attribute in a substrate, which is absurd and will be refuted later. The third is invalid because what is neither an attribute nor a substrate is distinct from the essence and cannot cause differences. Therefore, no external factor prevents the possibility of a body leaving its location. The passage cuts off at the end of the third alternative, leaving the conclusion implicit.

Key Terms
تمام الماهية
المتماثلات
اللوازم
الحجمية
الهيولى
الموصوفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think bodies differ intrinsically in their ability to move.
Correction: The proof shows that all bodies are essentially equal, so movement is equally possible for all bodies; no intrinsic difference prevents a body from leaving its location.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first proof that any body can leave its location?
A) Direct observation of moving bodies.
B) The equality of all bodies in their complete essence (tamām al-māhiyya).
C) The assumption that location is purely accidental.
D) The impossibility of hyle (matter).

Key Takeaway
Bodies are essentially equal, so no intrinsic difference prevents a body from leaving its location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this truth challenge your attachment to these things, and what one concrete step can you take today to loosen that grip?
?
The proof eliminates every possible cause of difference between bodies, leaving only the essence itself. If your worth does not lie in your body or its location, then what does it lie in? In what specific ways are you investing in that true worth today, rather than in temporary physical circumstances?
?
The possibility of leaving one's location is equally true for all bodies, meaning death (the soul's departure from the body) is a natural possibility. Are you living as if this possibility is real for you right now, or do you act as if you are permanently fixed in this world? What would change in your daily routine if you fully internalized that you could be moved at any moment?

Refuting Absolute Directions in Space
Author refutes the claim that bodies must occupy specific upper or lower places.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's refutation of the claim that bodies have intrinsic directions of above and below, and his argument that space outside the world is uniform and directionless.

Core Idea
"Above" and "below" are relative, not absolute — space outside the world has no direction.

In this passage, the author addresses a common misconception about the necessary positions of bodies in space. He argues that the concepts of above and below are relative to observers on earth, not intrinsic properties. The passage demonstrates a key principle of kalam cosmology: the infinite space outside the world is uniform and has no inherent directions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and locate the initial claim that some bodies must be in upper places and others in lower places.
This is the claim the author is refuting.
2
Follow the refutation
Notice how the author responds with 'this is extremely unlikely' and then provides his proof.
The proof is based on the nature of space outside the world.
3
Understand the relativity of directions
The author explains that above and below only exist relative to observers on earth after the world's creation. Outside the world, there is no such distinction.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the conclusion that no body is necessarily in a specific place; space is uniform.
This refutes the idea of intrinsic directional properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ الأجسام (۱) ] يجب حصولها في الأحياز الفوقانية ، والبعض الآخر يجب حصولها في الأحياز التحتانية ؟ فنقول : هذا في غاية البعد . وذلك لأنا قد دللنا على أن (٢) الفضاء خارج العالم ، لا نهاية له . فلما حصل هذا العالم (۳) صار بعض الأحياز بالنسبة إلى الواقفين على وجه الأرض فوقـاً وبعضها تحتاً . فأما خارج العالم فليس هناك فوق وتحت بل ليس هناك إلا الفضاء المتشابه الآخر في تمام الطبيعة والماهية . وذلك هو المطلوب
Some bodies must be in the upper places, and some others must be in the lower places? We say: this is extremely unlikely. That is because we have demonstrated that the space outside the world is infinite. When this world came to be, some places became 'above' relative to those standing on the face of the earth, and some 'below'. As for outside the world, there is no above or below; there is only the uniform space in complete nature and essence. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author presents a possible claim: that some bodies are necessarily in upper places and others in lower places. Then he refutes it by arguing that such a view is extremely unlikely. He explains that outside the world there is infinite space without any intrinsic direction. The concepts of above and below only arise relative to observers on earth after the world's creation. Therefore, the claim that bodies have a necessary place is false. The author's proof relies on the nature of infinite space being uniform.

Third Proof: Motion Possible via Simple and Compound Analysis
Author proves motion is possible for all bodies via analysis of simple and compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the analysis of simple and compound bodies demonstrates that motion is possible for all bodies.

Core Idea
Identical sides of simples imply motion for contact change.

This passage presents a sophisticated proof from the mutakallimun that motion is possible for all bodies, even simple ones. By analyzing the structure of bodies into simple parts with identical sides, the author shows that change in contact requires motion, establishing motion's possibility. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Classify bodies as simple or compound
Read the first part of the passage where the author divides bodies into simple (بسيط) and compound (مركب). Understand that a compound body ultimately consists of simples.
This classification is foundational to the argument.
2
Focus on simple bodies and their sides
The author argues that every simple body must have two sides (right and left) that are identical in essence. If they differed, the body would be compound, contradicting its simplicity.
This step uses the principle of contradiction.
3
Apply the principle of equality
Since the sides are identical in essence, whatever is valid for one side must be valid for the other. In particular, if the right side can be in contact, the left side can also be in contact.
This step relies on the principle that identical things have identical properties.
4
Conclude motion is possible
Change in contact (e.g., from right side touch to left side touch) is possible, as we observe in everyday life. Such change requires motion of the parts. Therefore, motion is possible for all bodies, including simples.
This is the desired conclusion (المطلوب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة على صحة الحركة على جميع الأجسام : أن نقول : [ الجسم ) ] إما أن يكون بسيطاً أو مركباً . فإن كان مركباً فكل مركب لا بد وأن ينتهي تحليل تركيبه إلى البسائط ، فوجب أن يكون ذلك المركب مركباً من الأشياء ، كل واحد منها [ في نفسه (٥) ] يكون بسيطاً ، وإذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : كل واحد من تلك البسائط، فإنه يجب أن يحصل له جانبان : أحدهما : يمينه . والآخر يساره . وطبيعة يمينه : لا بد وأن تكون مساوية الطبيعة يساره في تمام الماهية. وإلا لكان أحد هذين الجانبين مخالفاً للجانب الآخر في تمام الماهية. وحينئذ يكون ذلك الجسم مركباً، مع أنا فرضناه بسيطاً. هذا خلف . وإذا ثبت استواء جانبي ذلك الجزء في تمام الماهية ، فكل ما يصح على أحد ذينك الجانبين ، وجب أن يصح على الجانب الآخر . ضرورة أن المتساويات في تمام الماهية ، يجب استواؤها في جميع [ اللوازم (۱) ] وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الجسم الذي هو ممسوس يمينه، وجب أن يصح كونه ممسوساً يساره، وبالعكس. ولا يمكن حصول هذا المعنى ، إلا بحركة تلك الأجزاء . فلما كان التبدل في المماسة ممكناً ، ولا يحصل ذلك التبدل إلا عند حصول حركة الأجزاء ، وجب القول بأن تلك الحركة (٧) أيضاً : ممكنة وهو المطلوب
The third proof for the correctness of motion in all bodies: We say: The body is either simple or compound. If it is compound, every compound must have its composition analyzed down to simple things, so that compound must be composed of things each of which in itself is simple. If this is established, we say: Each one of those simples must have two sides: a right side and a left side. The nature of its right side must be equal to the nature of its left side in complete essence. Otherwise, one of these sides would differ from the other in complete essence, and then that body would be compound, whereas we assumed it simple. This is a contradiction. If it is established that the two sides of that part are equal in complete essence, then whatever is valid for one of those sides must be valid for the other, necessarily because things equal in complete essence must be equal in all concomitants. If this is established, we say: The body whose right side is touched must be valid to have its left side touched, and vice versa. This meaning cannot occur except by motion of those parts. Since change in contact is possible, and that change cannot occur except when motion of parts occurs, it becomes necessary to say that motion is also possible. That is the desired conclusion.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a proof (hujja) that motion is possible for all bodies. The author argues that every body is either simple or compound. If compound, it ultimately consists of simples. A simple body must have two sides, right and left, which are identical in essence. If they differed, the body would be compound, contradicting its simplicity. Since they are identical, what is true of one side is true of the other. Contact with the right side implies contact with the left side is also possible. Change in contact requires motion. Since change in contact is possible (we observe it), motion of the parts must also be possible. Therefore, motion is possible for all bodies. The proof uses the principle that identical things have identical properties.

جانب
يمين
يسار
مماسة
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simple bodies cannot move because they have no internal parts to allow motion.
Correction: The argument shows that even simple bodies can move because change in contact between their sides requires motion of the whole body.

Checkpoint

A) Because the body is compound.

Key Takeaway
The third proof establishes that motion is possible for all bodies by showing that even simple bodies have identical sides, allowing change in contact, which necessitates motion.

Fourth Proof: All Bodies Are Capable of Motion
Author uses a compulsion argument: both celestial and elemental bodies must be movable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth compelling proof that all bodies—celestial and elemental—are capable of motion.

Core Idea
Both celestial and elemental bodies prove that all bodies are receptive to motion.

The author presents a fourth proof, described as 'ilzami' (compelling). It forces the opponent to accept the conclusion from premises they already hold. The argument divides bodies into two categories—celestial and elemental—and shows that each category entails motion capability. This passage demonstrates how a simple classification can yield a powerful theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the argument structure
Read the passage and notice how it first divides bodies into celestial and elemental. Then examine how each type supports the conclusion that all bodies are movable.
This is a classic 'division and proof' structure.
2
Analyze the celestial case
The author says celestial bodies must be moving according to the philosophers. Reflect on why this premise is used: it relies on the opponent's own belief.
This makes the argument 'ilzami' (compelling).
3
Analyze the elemental case
For elemental bodies, the author points out that each part of an element can be moving. This is based on empirical observation.
Even if not always moving, the possibility is enough.
4
Synthesize the conclusion
Combine both premises: since both types are either necessarily or possibly moving, all bodies are receptive to motion. This conclusion challenges those who deny motion to bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : وهي الزامية. وهي أن الأجسام ، إما فلكية ، وإما عنصرية. أما الفلكيات فإنه يجب كونها متحركة ، على مذهب الفلاسفة . وأما العنصريات فإن كل واحد من أجزاء العنصر الواحد ، يمكن كونه متحركاً . وذلك يدل على أن جميع الأجسام قابلة للحركة
The fourth proof: it is a compelling one. It is that bodies are either celestial or elemental. As for celestial bodies, they must be moving according to the philosophers. As for elemental bodies, each one of the parts of a single element can be moving. That indicates that all bodies are receptive to motion.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a concise compelling proof (ilzami). The author divides bodies into two categories: celestial (falakiyya) and elemental ('unsuriyya). He notes that on the philosophers' view, celestial bodies are necessarily moving (in orbits). Elemental bodies—each part of an element—can be in motion (as we observe). Therefore, since both types are either necessarily moving or possibly moving, all bodies are capable of motion (qabila li al-haraka). The argument is 'ilzami' because it forces the opponent to accept the conclusion from premises they already hold (e.g., motion of celestial spheres).

Key Terms

عنصرية
متحركة
قابلة للحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think celestial bodies are fundamentally different from elemental bodies and thus not subject to the same motion reasoning.
Correction: The argument uses the opponent's own belief (philosophers' view) about celestial motion to show that even celestial bodies must be movable, so all bodies are similar in being receptive to motion.

Checkpoint

B) The philosophers' doctrine

Key Takeaway

Fifth Proof: Bodies Leave Their Places
Author proves bodies are originated by observing they leave their places and do not return.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth proof for the temporality of bodies based on their movement out of places and failure to revert.

Core Idea
Bodies leaving their places without returning shows they are contingent, not eternal.

The fifth proof of the author uses a simple observation: bodies move and do not revert to their original places. This seemingly trivial fact carries deep implications for the nature of physical bodies. In this lesson, we will examine this argument and see why it forces the conclusion that bodies are originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note the key terms used.
The passage is concise but packed with reasoning.
2
Understand the core reasoning
Identify the observation (bodies leave places), the second observation (they do not return), and the inference (they do not necessarily occupy specific places, hence they are contingent and originated).
The absence of necessary placement is crucial.
3
See the extension to all bodies
The author extends the conclusion to all bodies based on the principle of the likeness of bodies (tamathul al-ajsam).
This step is important for the universality of the proof.
4
Note the exclusion of anthropomorphists
The author points out that this proof cannot be used by those who attribute a physical place to Allah (Mujassima, Karamiyya), as it would be invalidated when applied to His essence.
This is a doctrinal boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : أن نقول : لا شك أن هذه الأجسام المحسوسة ، تخرج عن أحيازها ، وإذا خرجت عن أحيازها ، وزالت من إخراجها عن تلك الأحياز، فإنها لا تعتد بطابعها إلى الأحياز المتقدمة ، وذلك يدل على أن هذه الأجزاء لا يجب حصولها في أحياز معينة، وأنه يمكن خروجها عن تلك الأحياز المعينة ، وذلك كاف في إقامة الدليل على حدوث هذه الأجسام . ثم إذا ثبت هذا المطلوب في هذه الأجسام ، ثبت أيضاً في سائر الأجسام بالبناء على وجوب تماثل الأجسام . واعلم أن المجسمة والكرامية، وكل من زعم أن الله مختص بحيز معين ، وحاصل في جهة معينة، فإنه لا يمكنه تقرير هذه الدلائل البتة . لأنها بأسرها تصير منقوضة على قوله بذات الله تعالى
The fifth proof: we say: there is no doubt that these sensible bodies leave their places. When they leave their places and cease from being removed from those places, they do not revert by their nature to the previous places. That indicates that these parts do not necessarily occupy specific places, and that they can leave those specific places. That is sufficient to establish the proof for the temporality of these bodies. Then if this desired conclusion is established for these bodies, it is also established for all other bodies by building on the necessity of likeness of bodies. Know that the Mujassima and the Karamiyya, and everyone who claims that Allah is confined to a specific place and located in a specific direction, can by no means establish these proofs, because they all become invalidated by his statement concerning the essence of Allah, exalted.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof, concluding that bodies are originated (hadith). The author argues from empirical observation: bodies move out of their places and do not naturally return to their original positions. This shows they do not necessarily occupy fixed places; their locations are not necessary but accidental. From this, we infer that they must be originated (not eternal). This conclusion then applies to all bodies because they are all similar (mutamathil). The author adds a remark: this proof cannot be used by those who attribute a physical place to Allah (like the Mujassima and Karamiyya), because if they apply the same reasoning to Allah's essence, it would lead to an unacceptable conclusion. So the proof hinges on the contingency of bodies.

Proof that Rest is Originated and Objection about Its Fate
Author proves rest is originated and records an objection about its fate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that rest is originated and the objections opponents raise regarding its non-cessation.

Core Idea
Rest can cease; what can cease cannot be eternal — thus rest is originated.

The author presents a logical proof that rest (sukun) is a contingent, originated attribute. Since bodies can leave their places, the rest associated with those places must cease, so rest cannot be eternal. However, opponents object by suggesting that rest might not simply cease but could become latent, transfer to another body, or vanish into no location. This lesson introduces both the proof and the challenge, setting up the need for a refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument and flow.
Notice the structure: first the proof, then the objection.
2
Identify the key premise and conclusion
The proof hinges on two statements: 'rest is subject to cessation' and 'everything subject to cessation cannot be eternal.' Trace how these lead to the conclusion that rest is originated.
3
Examine the three counter-proposals
The objector offers three alternative fates for rest: becoming latent, transferring to another body, or going to no place. Identify each and consider why they might be problematic.
The author's response is not in this passage, but you can anticipate that these alternatives are rejected.
4
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this issue matters: if rest were eternal, it would conflict with the contingency of the world and the impossibility of multiple eternals.
This connects to broader kalam discussions on divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة في بيان أن السكون محدث: فنقول : الدليل عليه : أن السكون جائز الزوال ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فإنه يمتنع أن يكون أزلياً ، إنما قلنا : إن كل سكون جائز الزوال ، لأن كل جسم فإنه يصح خروجه عن حيزه ، وإذا خرج [ عن حيزه (۱) ] فإنه لا بد وأن يعدم حال كونه حاصلا في ذلك الحيز ، وذلك يقتضي أن كل سكون فإنه جائز الزوال . فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الجسم إذا خرج عن ذلك الحيز ، فإن ذلك السكون لا يصير معدوماً ، بل نقول : إنه يصير كامناً بعد أن كان ذلك ظاهراً ، أو يقال : إن : ذلك السكون ينتقل من ذلك الجسم إلى جسم آخر ، أو يقال : إنه ينتقل إلى لا في محل ؟
The third issue: clarifying that rest is originated. We say: the proof for it is that rest is subject to cessation, and everything that is subject to cessation cannot be eternal. We only say that every rest is subject to cessation because every body can leave its place. When it leaves its place, then it must become non-existent while it is present in that place. That necessitates that every rest is subject to cessation. If it is said: why is it not permissible to say that when a body leaves that place, that rest does not become non-existent, but rather we say: it becomes latent after it was apparent, or it is said that that rest transfers from that body to another body, or it is said that it transfers to no place?
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a third issue (mas'ala) demonstrating that rest (sukun) is originated (muhdath). The author gives a proof: rest can cease (since bodies can move away from a place, the rest associated with that place ends), and whatever can cease cannot be eternal; therefore rest is not eternal, hence originated. Then an objection is raised: why not say that rest does not become non-existent when the body moves, but rather becomes latent (kamin), or transfers to another body, or transfers to no location? The objector offers alternative possibilities to avoid concluding that rest is originated. The author's answer is not present in this window, but the objection is recorded. The argument shows that rest is not a permanent attribute but contingent.

The Twofold Answer on Rest and Motion
Author provides two answers to an objection on rest and motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold answer to the objection about rest and motion, and how redefining rest as a relation refutes the objection.

Core Idea
Rest is not a thing; it is just a relation, so when the body moves, the relation simply ceases.

In this passage, the author presents two distinct responses to an objection against the eternity of the world. The first answer redefines 'rest' as a mere relation, showing that no underlying entity persists when a body moves. The second answer concedes the opponents' framework but shows it still leads to the same conclusion. Let's examine each answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Focus on how the author defines rest as a relation and what follows when the body leaves the space.
Notice the self-evident nature of this.
2
Read the second answer
See how the author accepts the opponents' assumptions but still derives the argument against eternity.
3
Compare the approaches
Consider why two answers are given and how they differ in strategy.
The first is more minimalist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب من وجهين : الأول : إن المراد من السكون : هو مجرد حصول الجسم في ذلك الحيز ، وهذا الحصول لا معنى له إلا هذه النسبة المخصوصة ، وهذه الإضافة المخصوصة . فإذا خرج الجسم عن ذلك الحيز ، فقد بطل كونه حاصلا فيه لأن الإضافات إذا لم تبق ، فقد عدمت وفنيت ، وزالت . والعلم به ضروري بل لو ادعينا : إثبات معنى يستقل بنفسه ، يوجب كون الجسم حاصلا في الحيز ، فهذا السؤال متوجه عليه . والوجه الثاني في الجواب : إنا لو سلمنا صحة الكمون والظهور ، وصحة الانتقال على الأعراض. إلا أنا نقول : لو كان الجسم أزلياً ، لكان في الأزل . إما أن يكون حالة واحدة من هذه الأحوال من غير تغير وتبدل . وإما أن تكون موصوفة بالتبدل والتغير، ويسمى بقاء الجسم على حالة واحدة بالسكون ، انتقاله ويسمى من حالة إلى حالة أخرى بالحركة. وحينئذ يتمشى الدليل المذكور . مسائل : فيثبت : أن هذا السؤال ساقط بسبب هذين الوجهين : والعجب : أن المتكلمين طولوا في هذا الباب ، وتكلموا في أربع إحداها : في إبطال الكمون والظهور . وثانيها : في إبطال انتقال الأعراض من محل إلى محل آخر . وثالثها : في انتقال الأعراض من محلها ، إلى لا في محل . رابعها : بيان أن العرض لا يقوم بالعرض . وإنما خاضوا في تقرير هذه المسائل الأربع، لدفع السؤال المذكور . وقد ظهر بالتقرير الذي ذكرناه : أنه لا حاجة البتة في دفع ذلك السؤال إلى شيء من هذه المسائل : فظهر : أن الخوض فيها فضول من غير فائدة
And the answer is from two aspects: The first: that the intended meaning of 'rest' is merely the body's being in that space, and this 'being' has no meaning except this specific relation and this specific addition. So when the body leaves that space, its being in it becomes invalid, because if the relations do not remain, they have ceased and perished and vanished. And knowledge of this is necessary. Rather, if we claim the affirmation of an meaning independent in itself that necessitates the body's being in the space, then this question is directed against it. And the second aspect in the answer: that even if we concede the validity of latent and manifest, and the validity of transfer of accidents, yet we say: if the body were eternal, it would be in pre-eternity either in a single state among these states without change or alteration, or described by alteration and change. The body's remaining in one state is called 'rest', and its transfer from one state to another is called 'motion'. And then the mentioned proof proceeds. Issues: It is established that this question is invalid due to these two aspects. And it is remarkable that the theologians lengthened discussion in this chapter and spoke about four things: First: invalidating latent and manifest. Second: invalidating the transfer of accidents from one locus to another. Third: the transfer of accidents from their locus to what is not a locus. Fourth: demonstrating that an accident does not subsist in an accident. They only delved into establishing these four issues to repulse the mentioned question. And it has become clear by the exposition we have mentioned that there is absolutely no need in repelling that question for anything from these issues. So it becomes clear that delving into them is superfluous without benefit.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage presents a two-fold answer to an objection concerning the nature of rest and motion and the changes in bodies. The author first redefines 'rest' as merely a relation of the body to a specific space; when the body leaves that space, the relation ceases to exist, and there is no underlying entity that persists. This is self-evident. The second answer concedes the traditional concepts of latent/manifest and transfer of accidents, but argues that if the body were eternal, it would have to remain unchanged (rest) or change (motion), and the proof against its eternity would still hold. After these two answers, the author expresses astonishment that theologians have elaborated on four issues (invalidating latent/manifest, transfer of accidents, etc.) to deal with the objection, when the two simple answers show that those discussions are unnecessary and without benefit. The passage thus critiques over-complication in theological argumentation.

الإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Rest is only a relation; when the spatial relation ceases, so does 'rest'. No additional entity is needed.

Checkpoint

A) An intrinsic property of the body
B) A specific relation to a space
C) The absence of motion
D) A latent quality that appears

Key Takeaway
The first answer shows that rest is not an independent entity but a relation, refuting the objection without extra assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If rest is just a relation, then your spiritual motion or stagnation is not an unchangeable nature but a series of relations. Are you willing to take responsibility for changing your relationship with Allah, rather than blaming a fixed 'state'?

The Superfluity of the Four Issues
Author critiques the four issues theologians discuss and declares them unnecessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the four issues (latent/manifest, transfer of accidents, etc.) unnecessary for refuting the objection.

Core Idea
The four issues debated by theologians are superfluous; the two answers already suffice.

After presenting the two answers, the author expresses astonishment at how theologians have lengthened the discussion by addressing four additional issues. He argues that these issues are completely unnecessary for repelling the original objection. Let's see what those issues are and why he deems them futile.

The Objection of Cessation in Change
First objection against proof of creation from change, with counter-argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the proof of creation from change, which questions whether change necessarily implies the cessation of something eternal.

Core Idea
Change does not automatically prove a beginning; it may only indicate a transition without cessation.

This lesson introduces a powerful objection against the classic argument that change in the world proves its temporal beginning. The objector challenges the very premise that change requires the removal of an eternal attribute. By analyzing the logical options, we will see why this objection is considered 'overwhelming' by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Read the opening question: Does negation/affirmation necessitate cessation and occurrence? This sets up the dilemma.
The objector is forcing a logical fork.
2
Explore the first fork
If change does imply cessation, then either the ceased thing is eternal (contradicting the opponent) or originated (leading to infinite regress).
3
Explore the second fork
If change does not imply cessation, then the original proof is invalid because movement after rest would not require any new occurrence.
This parallels the case of knowledge in the divine essence.
4
Appreciate the objection's strength
The author labels this a 'qahir' (overwhelming) objection. Consider why it is so effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النفي والإثبات هل يقتضي زوال أمر كان، وحصول أمر حادث، أو لا يقتضي؟ فإن كان الأول. فنقول: ذلك الذي زال. إن كان قديماً فقد اعترفتم بأن القديم قد زال. وإن كان حادثاً فالكلام في ذلك الحادث، كالكلام في الأول، ولزم أن يكون كل علم مسبوقاً بعلم آخر إلى أول. وإذا جاز هذا، فقد بطل دليلكم في إفساد القول بحوادث لا أول لها. وأما إن قلنا: إن صدق قولنا [ما كان عالماً بوجوده لا يقتضي حدوث ولا زوال شيء. فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن صدق قولنا] بتحرك الجسم بعد أن كان ساكناً، لا يقتضي أيضاً زوال شيء، ولا حدوث شيء. وحينئذ يبطل الدليل المذكور؟ فهذا سؤال قاهر، قوي على هذا الدليل.
Does negation and affirmation necessitate the cessation of a thing that existed, and the occurrence of a new thing, or not? If the first, then we say: that which ceased. If it was eternal, then you have admitted that the eternal has ceased. If it was originated, then the discussion about that originated thing is like the discussion about the first, and it would be necessary that every knowledge be preceded by another knowledge ad infinitum. And if this is permissible, then your proof for refuting the claim of occurrences without a beginning has been invalidated. And if we say: the truth of our statement [he was not knowing by His existence does not necessitate occurrence nor cessation of anything. Then why is it not permissible to say: the truth of our statement] concerning the movement of the body after it was at rest does not also necessitate the cessation of anything nor the occurrence of anything? Then the mentioned proof is invalidated. This is an overwhelming, strong objection against this proof.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against the proof of creation based on change. The objector begins by asking whether a change from nonexistence to existence (negation to affirmation) necessarily implies the removal of something that existed and the emergence of something new, or not. If it does imply this, then the thing that is removed: if it is eternal, then the opponent has admitted that the eternal can cease; if it is originated, then an infinite regress of knowledge would be required, which would undermine the opponent's own argument against an infinite series of temporal events. Then the objector offers an alternative: if one claims that the truth of the statement 'he was not knowing by His existence' does not require any occurrence or cessation, then similarly the statement about a body moving after being at rest does not require any cessation or occurrence. Thus the original proof is invalidated. The passage ends by labeling this as a strong and overwhelming objection.

Key Terms
النفي والإثبات
زوال
قديم
حادث
حوادث لا أول لها
السكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector is denying change itself.
Correction: The objector is not denying change itself but questioning whether change necessarily proves a beginning for the world.

Checkpoint
What does the objector argue would happen if the first option (change implies removal) is adopted and the removed thing is eternal?
A) The eternal cannot cease, so the option is false.
B) The opponent has admitted that the eternal can cease.
C) An infinite regress of knowledge follows.
D) The proof of occurrences without a beginning becomes stronger.

Key Takeaway
This objection cleverly turns the proof of creation from change against itself by showing that either the premise leads to an unacceptable conclusion or the proof becomes invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The objector here presents a powerful argument that could shake the foundation of a proof for God's existence. ?
The objector uses an analogy: just as the statement about God not knowing does not imply change, so movement after rest does not imply change. Have you ever applied a double standard in your reasoning—accepting certain premises in one area but rejecting them in another? Where in your life do you need to be more consistent in your thinking about God and the world?
?
This objection shows that even a seemingly solid proof can be challenged. Does the possibility that your intellectual foundations might be flawed frighten you? Why? How does your ultimate trust rest—on arguments or on the One to whom they point?

The Two Meanings of 'Not Prior'
Clarifying the meaning of 'not prior' to refute an argument about number priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how distinguishing between real priority and mental priority refutes an argument about number.

Core Idea
An argument's premise may be ambiguous, and ambiguity can conceal a logical error.

The author now responds to the fifth argument concerning priority. He clarifies that the claim "no number is prior to another" can mean two very different things: either nothing in reality has priority, or our minds simply do not perceive priority. This distinction is crucial for evaluating the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two interpretations introduced.
The author references the science of logic.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find the phrases 'in reality itself' (نفس الأمر) and 'in the mind and thought' (الذهن والخاطر). Understand the difference.
3
See the consequences
Note that the first meaning is denied (ممنوع), and the second only leads to hesitation (توقف) and lack of certainty.
This shows the argument does not prove its point.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Understand why this ambiguity defeats the fifth argument: the premise is not decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الحجة الخامسة : إنا بينا في علم المنطق : إن قول القائل : إنه ليس عدد أولى من عدد : إذا أريد به عدم الأولوية في نفس الأمر ، فهذا ممنوع ، وإن أريد به عدم الأولوية في الذهن والخاطر ، فهذا لا يفيد إلا التوقف وعدم الجزم . والله أعلم
And the answer to the fifth argument: we have explained in the science of logic that the saying of someone: 'It is not that number is more deserving than number' – if what is meant by it is the lack of priority in reality itself, then this is prevented; and if what is meant by it is the lack of priority in the mind and thought, then this only leads to hesitation and lack of certainty. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage answers the fifth argument about priority. The author references logic: the statement 'it is not that number is more deserving than number' can be interpreted in two ways. If it means no priority in reality, that is denied; if it means no priority in the mind, that only leads to hesitation (tawaqquf) and lack of certainty. So the argument does not prove its point.

Key Terms
أولوية
نفس الأمر
ذهن
خاطر
توقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The statement 'no number is prior to another' is straightforward and decisive.
Correction: The statement is ambiguous: it could refer to real priority (which is not necessarily true) or mental priority (which only indicates uncertainty).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's response to the statement 'It is not that number is more deserving than number'?
A) He accepts it as a proof that all numbers are equal.
B) He argues it is false without qualification.
C) He shows it has two possible meanings, neither of which proves the argument.
D) He says it leads to certainty about priority.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument fails because its key premise is ambiguous and not decisive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author immediately turns to logic to dissect an argument. When you are presented with a compelling argument that challenges your faith, do you rush to accept or reject it, or do you first analyze its structure and assumptions? Commit today to learning the tools of logical analysis so you can follow the example of the scholars.

The Judging Power Must Be Both Receptive and Active
Al-Razi analyzes the judging power to show it is both receptive and agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that any power that judges must first have conceptualized the subject and predicate, making it both receptive and active.

Core Idea
Judgment without prior conceptualization is impossible.

Al-Razi presents the eighth proof from the judging power. He examines whether the power that affirms or negates a predicate of a subject has perceived the essences of those terms. His analysis reveals that the same power must be receptive to conceptual forms and then active in combining them. This lesson unpacks that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Let the text speak for itself before diving into commentary.
Notice the logical structure: premise, question, two alternatives, conclusion.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms: قوة حاكمة (judging power), إيجاب/سلب (affirmation/negation), تصديق (assent), تصور (conceptualization). Understand their roles.
These terms are central to Islamic logic and epistemology.
3
Trace the two alternatives
Al-Razi asks: Did the power perceive the essences of subject and predicate? If yes, then it received the forms and actively combined them. If no, then assent would occur without conceptualization, which is impossible.
The impossibility of the second alternative is the key to the proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since assent without prior conceptualization is impossible, the judging power must have perceived the essences. Therefore, the same power is both receptive (قابلة) and active (فاعلة).
This shows that conceptualization and judgment are intertwined.
5
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this matters: it establishes that all judgments rely on prior concepts, a foundational point in Islamic epistemology and theology.
This proof is part of Al-Razi's larger argument about the nature of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة : إن القوة التي تحكم بإيجاب المحمول على الموضوع أو بسلبه عنه، لا شك أنها فاعلة. فإن هذا الحكم فعل، فهذه القوة الحاكمة، هل أدركت ماهية الموضوع وماهية المحمول أم لا؟ فإن أدركت، فهل قبلت هاتين الصورتين، ثم أسندت إحداهما إلى الأخرى؟ فالقوة الواحدة قابلة لتلك التصورات، وفاعلة لأجل ذلك الحكم. وإن قلنا: إن ذلك المصدق، ما حضر عنده ماهية الموضوع وماهية المحمول، كان هذا تصديقاً بدون التصور، وإنه محال.
The eighth: Indeed the power that judges by affirming the predicate of the subject or negating it from it – there is no doubt that it is agent. For this judgment is an act. This judging power: did it perceive the essence of the subject and the essence of the predicate or not? If it perceived, then did it accept these two forms, then attribute one to the other? So the single power is receptive to those conceptualizations and agent for that judgment. And if we say that the assenter did not have present with him the essence of the subject and the essence of the predicate, then this would be assent without conceptualization, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage focuses on the judging power (al-quwwa al-ḥākima) that affirms or denies a predicate of a subject (i.e., judgment or taṣdīq). Al-Razi notes that judgment is an act, so the power is an agent. He then asks whether it has perceived the essences of subject and predicate. If it has, then it must have received those forms (tasawwurāt) and then actively combined them. Thus, the same power receives (qabūl) and judges (fi‘l). If it has not perceived them, then it judges without any conceptualization, which is impossible (muḥāl). Therefore, the judging power must be both qābil and fā‘il.

Key Terms
قوة حاكمة
إيجاب
سلب
تصديق
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is about court judgments or legal rulings.
Correction: Al-Razi is discussing the psychological or logical power that performs acts of affirmation and negation in reasoning, not legal judgments.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi say would be impossible for the judging power?
A) To affirm without negating.
B) To judge without conceptualization of subject and predicate.
C) To perceive the subject without the predicate.
D) To have multiple judgments.

Key Takeaway
The judging power cannot function without prior conceptualization of the terms involved, so it must be both receptive and active.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a judgment about something—whether in faith, knowledge, or daily life—do you first ensure you have truly conceptualized the subject and predicate? Or do you often affirm or deny without full understanding? How might this lead to false beliefs? Commit to verifying your concepts before passing judgment.
?
Al-Razi argues that judgment without prior conceptualization is impossible. Reflect on a time you made a hasty judgment about a person or idea without truly understanding it. How did that affect your actions? What can you do today to practice conceptualization before judgment?
?
The judging power in you is both receptive and active. Are you actively receiving truth before acting on it? Or do you act without receiving? This lesson calls you to be both a humble receiver and a responsible actor in your judgments. Which aspect do you neglect? Pledge to strengthen the weaker one.

Classification of Existents into Four Categories
Classification of existents into four categories based on agency and passivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of existents based on their capacity to act and be acted upon, and how this classification forms a foundation in philosophical theology.

Core Idea
Everything that exists falls into one of four roles: agent only, patient only, both, or neither.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents a fundamental classification of all existents according to their ability to affect and be affected. Before delving into deeper arguments, the author establishes this conceptual framework to distinguish between the Creator, matter, soul, and abstract realities like void and time. This classification is not merely descriptive; it underlies theological discussions about causation and dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the symmetrical structure: four categories defined by two criteria.
2
Identify the four categories
List each category and its description. Pay attention to the Arabic terms: مؤثر, متأثر, etc.
3
Understand the soul's dual role
The soul is unique in being both affected and agent. Reflect on why the author includes this middle category.
This category bridges the divine and material realms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الأولى
قالوا : الموجود إما أن يكون مؤثراً لا يتأثر، وهو البارى تعالى . أو متأثراً لا يؤثر ، وهو الهيولى . أو يكون متأثراً ومؤثراً وهو النفس . فإنها تقبل الأثر عن عالم الإله ، وتقوى على التأثير في الهيولى . وإما أن لا يكون مؤثراً ولا متأثراً ، وهو الفضاء والدهر .
The First Introduction. They said: The existent is either an agent that is not affected, and that is the Creator, exalted. Or affected and not an agent, and that is matter (hayula). Or affected and an agent, and that is the soul; for it receives influence from the divine world and has power to influence matter. Or neither an agent nor affected, and that is void and time.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of existents into four categories based on their capacity to act and be acted upon. The author states that existents are either: (1) an agent that is not affected, which is the Creator; (2) affected but not an agent, which is matter (hayula); (3) both affected and an agent, which is the soul; or (4) neither agent nor affected, which is void and time. It explains that the soul receives influence from the divine realm and can affect matter. This classification is a foundational division in philosophical theology.

Key Terms
مؤثر
متأثر
البارى
الهيولى
النفس
الفضاء
الدهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This classification is meant to be an exhaustive list of all types of existents in reality.
Correction: The classification is based on a specific definition of agency and passivity; it is a conceptual tool for philosophical theology, not necessarily an exhaustive metaphysical inventory.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which category includes both agency and passivity?
A) The Creator
B) Matter
C) The soul
D) Void and time

Key Takeaway
Existents are divided into four categories: pure agent (Creator), pure patient (matter), both (soul), and neither (void/time).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a soul: both affected and an agent. When you last felt drawn to something higher, did you recognise it as your soul receiving from the divine? Or did you dismiss it as mere emotion? Today, will you deliberately open yourself to that influence and then act on it?
?
The Creator is the only pure agent, yet you often act as if you control outcomes. Where in your life have you assumed the role of the Creator—trying to force results without reliance on Him? How can you return to being a receptive soul this week?
?
Void and time are neither agents nor affected. Does your life include empty spaces where you are not engaging as a soul—neither receiving from God nor acting on His behalf? What one void will you fill with purposeful reflection today?

The Intoxicated Person Analogy: Soul's Love for the Body
Analogy comparing soul's love to intoxication cycle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogy of the intoxicated person and how it illustrates the soul's attachment to bodily pleasures despite moments of rational clarity.

Core Idea
The soul's love for the body is like a drunkard who momentarily sees truth but returns to intoxication.

In this passage, the author presents a vivid analogy to explain a subtle point about the human soul. By comparing the soul's attachment to bodily pleasures to a drunkard's cycle of intoxication and brief clarity, he makes the concept tangible. This analogy helps us understand why the soul repeatedly falls back into worldly desires despite moments of rational insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the analogy's flow.
Notice the vivid imagery of the drunkard's state.
2
Identify the analogy's elements
Break down the analogy: what does the drink represent? The drunkard? The light of reason? The momentary awakening?
Each element maps to a part of the human experience with bodily pleasures.
3
Trace the cycle
Follow the sequence: intoxication, brief return of reason, then immediate relapse. How does this mirror the soul's behavior?
The cycle repeats; the soul never fully escapes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Read the author's concluding sentence: 'This is the explanation of the soul's love for the body.' Do you see why reflection confirms it?
The analogy is meant to be intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطيبات الجسمانية، جرى مجرى ما إذا تناول قدحاً عظيماً من الشراب المسكر فيصير كالسكران الطافح، المخبل الغافل. ثم بعد انقضاء ساعة طويلة ربما عاد إليه نور العقل، فيظهر للنفس قبائح هذا الشراب، وفضائحه في تلك اللحظة اللطيفة، ثم ربما ظهر له اشتغال بنوع آخر من هذه اللذات الجسمانية، فيكون ذلك كما إذا فتح السكران عينه لحظة، ورأى بعض الأشياء، وسمع بعضها. ثم إنه في الحال تناول قدحاً آخر، فعاد إلى غيبته المتقدمة، وغفلته الأولى. فهذا شرح عشق النفس على البدن، ولا شك أن من تأمل فيه، علم أن الأمر كذلك.
The bodily pleasures proceed in the manner of one who takes a huge cup of intoxicating drink and becomes like a raging, foolish, heedless drunkard. Then after a long hour passes, the light of reason may return to him, and the ugliness of this drink and its scandals appear to the soul in that delicate moment. Then perhaps he becomes occupied with another type of these bodily pleasures, so that is like when the drunkard opens his eye for a moment and sees some things and hears some of them. Then immediately he takes another cup and returns to his previous unconsciousness and first heedlessness. This is the explanation of the soul's love for the body, and there is no doubt that whoever reflects on it knows that the matter is so.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is an analogy. The author compares the human soul's love for bodily pleasures to a drunkard who occasionally regains consciousness but quickly falls back into intoxication. The bodily pleasures are like the drink that intoxicates the soul, causing it to forget its true nature. The 'light of reason' momentarily shows the soul the ugliness of these pleasures, but the soul soon becomes occupied with another pleasure and returns to its heedless state. The author concludes that this analogy explains the soul's love for the body, and reflection confirms it.

Key Terms
عشق النفس
نور العقل
الطيبات الجسمانية
السكران الطافح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The analogy denigrates the body as inherently evil.
Correction: The analogy is not about the body's evil but about the soul's excessive attachment to bodily pleasures, which leads to heedlessness.

Checkpoint
In the analogy, what does the 'light of reason' reveal to the soul?
A) The beauty of bodily pleasures
B) The ugliness and scandals of the drink
C) The need to drink more
D) The soul's true origin

Key Takeaway
The soul's love for the body is a cycle of heedless indulgence, brief rational clarity, and immediate relapse, as illustrated by the drunkard analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you indulge in a bodily pleasure, do you ever stop to see its ugliness in the light of reason, and what do you do in that delicate moment before you reach for another cup?
?
The passage says the drunkard sees and hears some things in that moment of clarity. What have you seen or heard in your moments of spiritual awareness that you immediately ignored? What will you do differently the next time that moment returns?
?
If the soul's love for the body is like this cycle, how can you break the cycle rather than just acknowledge it? What specific step will you take today to lengthen that moment of reason and not return to heedlessness?
